NICARAGUA
AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
SU1MARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises an agricultural credit project to support a
three-year lending program  for finaancing investments on about 550 ranches/
farms under five major categories:  rainfed dairy, irrigated dairy, rainfed
beef breeding and fattening; beef fattening on irrigated pastures; and ir-
rigated crops.  Agriculture is important to Nicaragua, contributing 70%
of the country's exports and 25% of its GDP and employing 60% of the labor
force. Conditions essential to growth in this sector are acceleration of
the on-going process of diversification away from the traditional export
crops such as coffee, and improvement of the technical effi.ciency and expan-
sion of agricultural production, especially livestock, for which the country
is well suited by its natural resources but inadequately equipped iD. term5
of credit and extension.  The project seeks to meet these needs part:1y 'y
providing long-term credit and supporting technical assistance to sub-
borrowers in a spectrum of ranch/farm sizes.
ii.        This would be the fifth Bank loan to Nicaragu.a in the agricu tural
sector. The first three, made in the 1950's for grain silos and machinery,
were almost fully disbursed but the fourth, approved in 1963 for financing
an irrigation project, remained largely undisbursed and was later cancelled,
mainly because of an unforeseen rise in project cost.
iii.       The proposed loan of US$8.5 million to the the Central Bank of
Nicaragua (CB) on the guarantee of the Republic of Nicaragua would finaace
54% (which is almost equal to the foreign excharige component) of total proj ect
cost, estimated at US$15.8 iaillion equivalent.  The Special Development F nd
(FONDO), owned by Government and administered by the CB, Swold contribut 
US$1.6 million equivalent, or about 10% of the total cost, while the baldnce
of about 36% would be contributed in equal shares by the participating bas ks
and the sub-borrowers. Of the project cost, 26% would be for rainfed dairy
farms; 12% for irrigated dairy farms; 26% for rainfed beef breeding and
fattening ranches; 18% for irrigated beef fattpening unIts; and '12% for irrigated
crop farms.  The balance would be for supporting and tech nical servIces.
iv.        The period of sub-loans would vary from five years for irrigatsd
beef fattening to 1 2 years for rainfed beef. Loans to relatively small
beneficiaries would be charged interest at i0.25% and others would p1ay 72-,$
made up of 11% interest and an appraisal and suparvision fee of 1%
While the FONDO would earn a spread only on loans to sub-borrowers other
than smal'l beneficiaries, participating banks would retain a larger awiouiat
(3%) on loans to small sub-borrowers than on those to others (2.50%)..  Other
features of the project aimed at ensuring that project lending would not be
restricted to a few large ranchers would be (a) the smaller contributions



- ii 
required from banks and small beneficiaries to projects of such sub-borrowers;
(b) the stipulation that no sub-loan would be made by a participating bank to
a,ixS sub-borrower in excess of US$100,000; (c) the condition that the annual
pKToportion of loans to small beneficiaries to total project loans made would
be at least 15% in the first year, 20% in the second year and 25% in the third
year, and (d) the reservation of US$1.5 million out of the Bank loan proceeds
for such sub-borrowers.
v.        The project would be administered by the Central Ba-nk for the FONDO
through a Special Project Unit.  The FONDO would finance sub-borrowers through
the Government-owned Banco Nacional de Nicaragua (BN), the largest bank in. thte
country and the major institutional source of agricultural credit, and through
six private commercial banks whose modest role in agriculture has so far beer
restricted mainly to production credit.  The Special Project IUnit, under an
internationally recruited Technical Director and a Nicaraguan Co-Director,
would employ its own technicians and also guide those engaged by the banks to
appraise and supervise their sub-loans.  The project would strengthen BN's
current efforts to improve its financial position.
vi.       Items to be financed are varied and would not be suitable for bulk
procurement th'rough international competitive biddirng.  Farmers/ranchers
would make their purchases through normal co7.ercial channels, which prow-de
adequate competition and supplies and represent various international firmz.
Vehicles and supplies costing $20,000 or more which are required for tech.nical
services would be procured on the basis of quotations invited through local
advertizing with notification to embassies to assure international competition.
vii.      Estimated financial rates of return range from  17% to 40% and the
estimated overall economic rate of return is 25%X   With appropriate assur-
ances, the project would be suitable for a Bank loan. of US$&85 million re-
payable in 16 years, including five years of grace. The borrower wou-ld be
the Central Bank of Nicaragua and the loan would be guaranteed by the Re-
public of Nicaragua.



NI CARAGUA
AGRICULTURAL CREIDIT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      Despite reserves of natural resources and the enterprise of com-
mercial farmers raising established crops, agricultural development in
Nicaragua has been handicapped by the lack of suitable term lending facil-
ities, especially from commercial banks, and the inadequacy of extension
services. The proposed project is designed to help remedy these weaknesses
by providing medium- and long-term  credit, together with supporting technical
assistance, for financing investment in the livestock and crop sub-sectors
of Nicaraguan agriculture. A loan of US$8.5 million to the Central Bank of
Nicaragua (CB) on the guarantee of the Republic of Nicaragua is recommended.
1.02      The Bank has made four loans to Nicaragua in the agriculutural
sector: (A) NI-52 of US$550,000 in 1951 for construction of grain silos;
(b) NI-44 of US$1,200,000 in 1951 to Banco Nacional de Nicaragua (Bli') for
purchase of agricultural machinery; (c) NI-130 of US$1,500,000 in 1955 to
Instituto de Fomento Nacional (INFONAC) for equipment, machinery, and sup-
plies for opening up new land, pasture improvement, and purchase of breeding
stock and such; and (d) NI-332 of US$2,600,000 in 1963 for the Rivas Irriga-
tion Project. The first three were almost fully disbursed, but only US$0.12
million of the last loan was drawn, the balance being cancelled, mainly be-
cause of an unforeseen increase in project cost and the inability.to deter-
mine suitable high-value crops on the basis of available technical and ec-
onomic data.
1.03      The reports of two FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program missions which
visited Nicaragua in February/March 1972 and of a Bank pre-appraisal mis-
sion in July 1972 and further preparatory work done by. CB provided the
basis for this project. This report is based on the findings of an ap-
praisal mission to Nicaragua in January/February 1973, consisting of
Messrs. C.G. Ramasubbu, Frank Thomas, and J.,J  Romero-Chavez (Bank), and
R.A. Crofts (consultant) and C. Sandoval (Bank trainee from Nacional
Financiera, Mexico).
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2
2.01      Nicaragua has an area of 118,358 knm  and an estimated population
of about 2.1 million in 1972 (which is growing at about 3.2% per year),
representing an average density of about 18 per km2. The country is divided
0



-2-
into three distinct zones: the Pacific, the Atlantic, and the Central (Map),
The Pacific Zone, which extends broadly from the border wlth Honduras to the
western shore of Lake Nicaragua, is endowed with fertile soils and consistent
rainfall.  It is the most accessible in terms of roads and inland water rotates
and accounts for 60% of the population. The most sparsely populated and poor-
ly developed is the Atlantic Zone, which extends from the lower sloues of the
Central Highlands to the Atlantic Coast and is characterized by heavy rainfall,
tropical forests, and poor roads.  Between the two lies the Cerntral Zone with
its mountain ranges, small plateaus, and valleys of grasslands.  Despite ln-
creasing migration to urban areas - Managua alone accounting for about a fit th'
of the country's population - about 52% live in rural areas.
2.02      Because economic growth has been affected in the vast by cyclical
fluctuations in the world markets for a few primarv comnodities, export di-
versification has been a major goal of national pollcy.  These efforts have
helped cotton to replace coffee as Nicaragua's main export and, meat, tex-
tiles, and chemicals to emerge as new exports. Annual growth rate of GDP at
1958 market prices averaged 4.3% between 1966 and 1970 and the per capita
income reached US$436 by 1971 but the benefits of growth have not been wide-
ly dispersed.  The earthquake which rocked MTanagua in Decemiber 19?'2., has -re-
sulted in a loss estimated at about one-third of the 1972 GNP and about 10%
of the nation's capital stock.  Reconstructionv is now actively in progressn
supported by external assistance.
2.03      Nicaragua's overall balance of payments position is comfortable,.
thanks to high reserves, favorable prices for traditional exports and exter_-
nal aid.  Its balance of trade in agricultural products has been favorable,
desoite fluctuating export earnings resulting from world Tmarket trends.   a
eluding sugar and meat, agricultural exports rose from USIJS49 million i,i 1960
to US$132 million in 1971 and US$163 million in 1972 (estimated).  Agricul-
tural imports (US$9 million in 1972) mainly included vegetab'les, maize,
plantains, and beans.  The 1972 drought has affected exports and increased
the need for imports, some of which, however, are being fi-nanced bv P-Xternal
grants .
B. Agricultural Sector
2.04      Agriculture is important to Nicaragua as, it accounts for about 25%
of GDP, nearly 70%. of exports, and about 50 to 60% of total employment.  Tlle
structure of agriculture is dualistic, with a modernized sector of large- and
medium-sized farms producing export-oriented crops such as cotton, coffee,
sugarcane, bananas and irrigated rice and beef and a traditional sector (en-
gaging about one-half of the farmers) producing maize, beans, sorghum, and
non-irrigated rice. Traditional farmers use little fertilizer, pesticides,
or machinery and produce under rainfed conditions mainly to meet domestic
consumnption requirements. During the off season, they serve as harvesting
labor on the coffee and cotton farms.



-3-
2.05      Modern farming is concentrated mainly in the Pacific Zone where it
is based on rich volcanic soils and supported by fertilizer supply services,
machinery dealers, and agricultural industries. The Central Zone has many
medium-sized farms, generally raising coffee and livestock, while the large
plain of the Atlantic Zone is undeveloped but shows good promise for live-
stock production.
2.06      Large tracts of arable land such as the natural grazing areas of
the open pine savanna in northeast Nicaragua are still not accessible but
new land has been progressively brought to use during the last two decades,
especially in the Central and Atlantic Zones. In 1971, about 40% of Nic-
aragua's total land area was in use, with nearly 50% of the 4.9 million ha
estimated to be under improved and natural pastures. The balance is used
for raising perennial and annual crops. Only about 6% of the cultivated
land (41,000 ha) was under irrigation in 1971 but substantial supplies of
underground water are understood to be available. A UNDP study, covering
about 158,400 ha in the Leon-Chinandega plain, estimated that, without deplet-
ing groundwater resources, 25,000 to 66,000 ha of this area could be irri-
gated annually, according to cropping pattern. Past irrigation schemes,
i however, have not been particularly successful, mainly because of the fail-
ure to develop crops justifying the high investment costs and in some cases
because of the use of unsuitable equipment.
2.07      Expanding irrigation and bringing additional land into use have
helped progress towards the Government's goals of food self-sufficiency and
increased and diversified exports.  The production of maize and beans has
been steadily rising over the last two decades and rice production has
doubled since 1963. Total agricultural exports have grown appreciably -
despite the ups and dowms of cotton - and beef and sugar have emerged as
two significant exports (Annex 1).  Goveriment has sought to support agri-
cultural developirent by keeping land and agricultural taxes low and pro-
viding an expanding volume of production credit.
2.08      Concentration of land holdings is an important feature of Nicaragua's
agrarian structure. About half the holdings are less than 7 ha and account
for 3.5% of the cultivated area. On the other hand, holdings above 350 ha
occupy 41.1% of the area, though they make up only 1.5% of the total number.
The Institute of Agrarian Reform (IAN), set up in 1963, has been concentrat-
ing on issue of land titles (about 15,000 up to end 1972) and extension of
land ownership through colonization schemes, assuming that redistribution
of holdings is not necessary at present in view of the progress being made
in opening up land for farm use. A major IAN project for bringing newq land
into production for transfer to landless peasants is the Rigoberto Cabezas
colonization scheme (350,000 ha) being developed with IDB assistance. IAN's
programs have generally been handicapped by lack of adequate funds and field
staff (Annex 1).
2.09      Official agricultural extension, education, and research are general-
ly deficient in Nicaragua, partly because of the insufficient allocation of



-4-
tunds to the Ministry of Agricu'lture for these purposes.  Except for improved
cotton seed varieties and control of diseases affecting that crop, research
has contributed little to Nicaraguan agriculture.  The benefits of -wiat has
been done have not reached most farmers because of the limited size of the
extension staff and the failure to link its activities with research, credit,
and marketing.  On the other hand, extension activities of BN (para 2.14),
mainly for the major export crops, and the INFONNAC (para 2. 15)i for tobaccco,
livestock, and fisheries, have had an impact, thanks to the larger nrmber of
technicians and their association with credit.  Also, an Argentine ecnsulzio'ng
firm (Latinoconsult).is providing valuable technical guidance to selected
farmners under a contract with CB.
2.10      The inadequacy of the rural infrastructure, including serviceas 3a'cn
as extension and marketing and facilities sucn as rural trananort, hae; partic
ularly handicapped the small farmers in participating in the gr!owh of
country' s crop and livestock sub-sectors.  Thus, it Is fotnC t'.at  thug;h t.".s
small farmer-oriented Credito Rural Program (implemented by BE  wth 7E  S
port) financed over 22,000 small farmers, mainly for their s                Lorttsxem
needs and helped increase production, it was badly handicaSpred in regard to
support in terms of marketing and technical services ard hence "Led to ' gh
delinquency (Annex 2, paras 33 to 35). Further, certain promisi.ng types
of on-farm  investment have not proved relevant for very small farmers.  In
the extensive area of inadequate and uncertain rainfall, the mini-mw-n viabe
size for a livestock unit tends to be larger than the average omall            ad.
the irrigated area (in which intensive cultivation could make Investment o.n 
a smal1 farm worthwhile) is only 670 of the total cultivated area and heI.d 3 nic"
by medium and large producers.  Identified investment op-portunities I)eing
thus limited, tne requirements of smxall farmers have been, mainly for proo-.jrn-
tilon credit and have been tackled as such unrder the Credito Rural Progrxxr

the rural infrastructure, it should be possible to formulate a eo':censive
program on the basis of the experience of the IDB-financed Credito Thural and
the modest schemes now in progress under the IAN7 after the resu..rs of the on-
going agricultural sector survey financed bv USA'-D are avaiiable at tie «d
of 1973.
C. Agricultural Credit
2. 1      CE has powers of control over the banking system in respect of in-
terest rates, legal reserves, credit ceilings, and such and is generally in
charge of the country's monetary policy, while the Superintendent of Banks
regulates banks' administrative procedures, credit management, and account-
ing and auditing stanadards (Annex 2).  The banking system consists of the               _



-5-
0             Government-owned BN and INFONAC, six private commercial banks (including
three foreign banks), four private finance companies, and other specialized
institutions dealing in insurance, housing finance, and storage.
2.12      By and large, private banks have played only a limited role in
agricultural credit, restricting their activities to short-term loans, mainly
for the major export crops, particularly cotton. In recent years, however,
they have made some medium-term loans for livestock but the amount has been
small (about 8% of the portfolio) and the period has been always less than
five years.  Private finance companies have not evinced active interest in
financing agriculture, making only a few loans to big ranchers and agro-
industrial units.  Farm loans have, therefore, come mainly from BN and, to
a lesser extent, from INFONAC (paras 2.14 and 2.15), Though their large
lerding has had a useful impact, the main sources of credit for most farmers
have been non-institutional, including landlords, processors of agricultural
produce, and relatives.
2.13      Bank credit to the commercial farming sector has helped expand
modernized production of cotton, coffee, sugarcane, rice, and beef, but
inadequacy of supporting technical assistance has been a serious weakness,
especially in credit programs for small farmers.  Government established
the Special Development Fund (FONDO) in 1972 under CB's administration to
encourage banking institutions to provide term loan facilities for specific
programs in diffe~rent sectors (paras 4001 and 4.02).
2.14      BN, as the major institutional source of agricultural credit, has
received funds from various foreign agencies, including USAID (US$16 million)
and the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB) (US$24 million) (Annex 2). Sup-
ported by 21 branches and a large technical department, the bank has advanced
loans for various economic activities, including substantial medium- and long-
term credit for agriculture and small industry under programs supported by
external loans. BN accounts for about two-thirds of all institutional agri-
cultural credit disbursed by the banking system, including the bulk of the
production credit needs of cotton, rice, beans, and sugarcane and term loans
for beef, dairy, and rice under various projects financed 'by IDB. While
these loans have generally gone to medium and large farmers, BN's Credito
Rural Program has been oriented to small farmers' requirements, especially
short-term production credit needs. In all, a sum of US$6.6 million equiva-
lent is outstanding to BN under this program, which has benefited about
22,000 farmers out of about 100,000 farmers in Nicaragua. BN's financial
position, however, has suffered over the years because of poor collections.
Additionally, BN's operating profits have been low because of various devel-
opmental expenses (for technical assistance under the Credito Rural program,
services of foreign experts, scholarships, research, etc.) incurred on behalf
of Government and it has not accumulated any substantial reserves.  More
recently, however, recovery performance has slightly improved, some bad debts
have been written off, and a small bad debt reserve has been built up ('para
4.07). Also, BN has periodically received additional resources from Govern-
ment and has attracted increasing deposits.
2.15      INFONAC, as a development institute, has wide-ranging functions,
including banking, technical assistance, and research in agriculture and



-6-
industry.  Its promotional role has been particularly related to pork, beef,
and milk production but its effectiveness as an agricultural credit institu-
tion has been limited by the inadequacy of technical staff, branch offices,
and counterpart funds; by liquidity problems; and by low profits.
III. TFiE PROJECT
A. Definition
3.01      The project would support a three-year lending program of the FONDO
to help finance on-farm development on five types of production units:  rain-
fed dairy farms, irrigated dairy farms, rainfed beef breeding and fatteirig
ranches, irrigated pastures and beef fattening farms, ano irrigated crop farms.
Loans would be made for periods between five and 12 years and would cover a
wide spectrum of farm/ranch sizes throughout the country, though the bulk of
the operations would be in the Pacific and Central Zones, Supporting and
technical services would also be provided.
3.02      The project would be administered by the Central Bank for the FONDO
through a Special Project Unit.  Proceeds of the Bank loan would be channel-
led through the FONDO to the BN and other participating banks and made avai:l
able to sub-borrowers on the basis of technically and financially evaluated
development plans.  Implementation of plans would be supervised by FONDO-
trained and approved technicians.
3.03      The project would provide a set of financial incentives to encour-
age relatively small investors to borrow and participating banks progressively
to increase loans to these. Small beneficiaries would be defined as those
whose major source of income is farming and the value of whose tctal assets
in farming (including farm machinery, livestock, and such, but excludiiag
land and any assets to be financed out of the sub-loan) does not exceed
US$20,000, and further, in the case of sub-borrowers for irrigated farming
for dairy or beef or crops, those whose irrigated holdings including area
to be irrigated with the sub-loan do not exceed 20 ha. Loans to such sub-
borrowers would be charged interest at 10.25% while others would pay 12% iade
up of 11% interest and an appraisal and supervision fee of 1% p.a. While the
FONDO would earn a spread only on loans to sub-borrowers other than small
beneficiaries, participating banks would retain a larger margin (3.0%) on
loans to small beneficiaries than on those to others (2.50%).  Smaller finan-
cial contributions would be required from banks and small beneficiaries to
projects of such sub-borrowers (Schedule A). Assurances were obtained that
the proportion of the amount of sub-loans to such small beneficiaries to the
total amount of sub-loans made under the project would be at least 15% during
the first 12-month period after Loan Agreement, 20% during the second, and
25% during the third. Further, at least US$1.50 million equivalent of the
total loan proceeds would be reserved for such beneficiaries. Moreover, to
ensure that the project funds are not restricted to a few large sub-borrowers,
the maximrium size of a sub-loan would be restricted to US$ 100,000 equivalent
(para 4.09)0

B.  Detailed Features
Rainfed Dairy Farms
3.05      Sub-loans in this category would be made to multi-purpose, rainfed
dairy farms in the Central Zone, especially in the Departments of Boaco and
Chontales. Major investment items would include land development, pasture
improvement through reseeding and use of fertilizer, fencing, stock water
supply, milking facilities, improved replacement heifers, and use of improved
dairy-type bulls or artificial insemination. The farm pattern would be
gradually converted to single-purpose milk production, and productivity would
be improved, through emphasis on animal health, parasite control, controlled
mating, and cow selection (Annex 3).
Irrigated Dairy Farms
3.06      These sub-loans would go mainly to small farms in the Leon Department
of the Pacific Zone where pasture production is limited by a sharply defined
dry season, running from December to May. Investment would be mainly in the
development of improved pastures based on irrigation, with rotational grazing



and fertilizer application.  Milkyards, bulk feed storage, and improved
milking cows and doiry-type bulls would also be financed.  Development would
aim at substantially increasing calving percentages, and milk productior, per
cow and per hectare (Annex 3).
Rainfed Beef Breeding and Fattening Farms
3,07      Sub-loans under this category would help accelerate the developm.ent
of beef ranches which already exist but receive little instituitional credit.
The aim wouild be to introduce appropriate legumes and grass mixtures and thus
iLprove the productivity of pastures, which has so far been restricted by
rainfall, soils, and poor management. Purchase of improved bulls, fenc-ing,
w,ater supplies, yards and dips, and access roads would also be f Inanceda
Through the improved nutritional status of pastures and better husbandry
ana management, the effective calving rate would be raised, adult mottalltv,
,mould be reduced, the carrying capacity would be doubled, and productiv7ity
wou ld be stepped up (Annex 3).
irrigated Pastures and Beef Fattening Farms
3.08      Sub-loarns under this category vould finance the establLshmment of
intensive fattening farms based on the purchase of steers and the developmetitn
of irrigated pastures, supported by access to local supplies of surface or
ground water.  These units, likely to be located mnainly in the Leon, Depar.ent           _
of the Pacific Zone, would help stratify the beef cattle industry, disperse
cattle fattening operations, and smooth out fluctuations in sUpPlv of steers.
Investments would help develop new areas likely to be served b' a I,own
irrigation source and include pasture establishment D electrical fevciug,
livestock, water supply, irrigation system, feed storage, and the Xl lk.  The
program should result in substantial increases in beef output, carrying capac-
ity, and livestock production (Annex 3)
3,09      Sub-loans for investment in irrigated ci-op farming wuld coer the
production of such crops as sugarcane; import substitutes, such as plantain
and onion; grains, particularly sorghum, to meet expanding demand for anienal
feedstuffs; and oi lseeds and fruit and vegetables where processing and market-
ing outlets can be assured (Annex 4).  No sub-loans wou'id be granted, however,,
for investiments involving mainly (a) cotton or coffee expansion, in view of;
the policy of diversification away from these crops and the fact that their
existing production is already largely capitalized; or (b) rice and tobacso5
since arrangements for their expansion have already been included in other
development programs.  Further, export prospects for rice are limited in
vie.w of their current subsidized prices. Investments would cover land
leveling; construction of wells, irrigation channels and on-farm storage;
4indbreark establishment; land preparation and planting (for perennial crops
only); tractor, trailer, and im.plement purchases; and installation of pumps
and a sprinkler system (where appropriate). A considerable range in invest-
ment costs is to be expected, although, in cases involving pumping, technical
conrsiderations would set the lower limit at around C$70,000 (US$10,000),  When



-9-
agricultural development priorities have been formulated on completion of
the on-going agricultural sector survey assisted by USAID, the crop com-
ponent would be reviewed by the Special Project Unit, taking into account
the project experience and any new investment opportunities in irrigated
or rainfed farming. Any modification of the exclusions detailed above
would, however, be referred to the Bank for prior approval. (Annex 4).
Supporting Services
3.10      Participating banks would make two loans to cattlemen's associa-
tions or cooperatives to enable them to provide joint services to ranchers or
farmers such as transporting milk and transporting and distributing fertil-
izers, animal feeds, and other requisites of the livestock sub-borrowers, after
ensuring that any livestock rancher in the area of operation of the associa-
tion or the cooperative is free to join its membership. Investments would
cover bulk storage sheds and transport trucks. Participating banks would also
make two loans available to contractors for the purchase of medium-heavy earth-
moving and ancillary equipment so as to assist sub-borrowers in cazrying
out the operations called for in the ranch/farm investment plans.  Assurances
were obtained that each of these four sub-loans would be made only with prior
Bank approval, based on the supply of full data regarding the sub-loans and
\the sub-borrowers to the Bank.
Technical Services
3.11      Expert Services and Equipment.  The Bank loan would finance the
foreign exchange cost of project administration, since present FONDO staff
is not adequate (para 4.03).  The Special Project Unit to be established in
FONDO would consist of a Technical Director, to be recruited internationally,
and a Nicaraguan team consisting of a Co-Director, a crops specialist, a
project analyst, and two project technicians (paras 4.03 and 4.04).
3.12      Beef Marketing Study.  The project would also provide for the
foreign exchange cost of a limited study of beef marketing, which would
be oriented to the export market and the provision of price incentives
for quality beef. Assurances were obtained that CB would arrange for the
study to be carried out by a consultant approved by the Bank according to
tenms of reference also approved by the Bank (para 5.04).
3.13      Other Services.  The project would, in addition, cover the foreign
exchange cost of establishing three activities to be organized with the
assistance of the Ministry of Agriculture, and possible cooperation from
cattlemen's associations:  (a) a scheme for the importation and multiplication
of improved grass and legume seeds (investment cost of US$125,000); two simple
marketing centers to provide decentralized services to ranchers wishing to
sell or buy cattle (investment cost of US$40,000); and (c) an artificial in-
semination center (investment cost of US$50,000) (Annex 3). Assurances were
obtained that the plans for these three activities would be furnished to the
Bank for its approval before they are implemented. It would be a condition
of loan effectiveness that the CB and the Ministry of Agriculture have made
the necessary arrangements for these three activities.

3.17      The Central Bank of Nicaragua would be the borrower and would c1nanael,
through the FONDO, the proceeds of the loan and the FONDO's own contributIick
to participating banks, which would, in turn, lend to farmers.  The 1Kepublic
of Nicaragua would guarantee the loan. The Republic would also undertake to
hold the CB harmless for any exchange losses attributable to the servicing of
the loan and make arrangements with CB for this purpose, as a cond4tion of
loan effectiveness.  The Bank loan would be at the standard interest rate
and repayable over a period of 16 years, including five years of grace., With
sub-loans being made over a three-year period, the last repayme-nt to the
participating banks and the FONDO would be made 15 years after the commencement
of the project (Annex 6).
D. Procurement and Disbursement
Procurement
3.18      As the items to be financed by sub-loans are varied and would be
purchased by sub-borrowers over a period of five years, they would not be
suitable for bulk procurement through international competitive bidding.
A large proportion of the investment goods to be financed will have to be
imported but such imports are generally unrestricted.  Rates of tax are low -



- 13 -
5% ad valorem import duties on fertilizers, pesticides, and irrigation and
other agricultural equipment and 7% on agricultural tractors.  In addition,
some small sales taes and cesses, amounting in all to 2.2% of landed costs,
are levied. Various international firms are represented in the country and
supplies and competition are adequate. After-sales services are also satis-
factory. Farmers would, therefore, procure their requirements through normal
local commercial channels. Land development, construction of farm buildings
and such would be carried out mostly by the farmers themselves or through
small local contractors. Such vehicles and equipment and supplies estimated
to cost US$ 20,000 or more as are required for the technical services connected
with the Project would be procured through normal commercial channels on the
basis of quotations to be invited through local advertising, with notification
to embassies to ensure international competition.
Disbursement
3.19      The Bank loan disbursement would take about four and a half years
(Annex 7). Disbursements would be made for 67% of sub-loans disbursed by
patticipating banks to small beneficiaries, 63% of all other sub-loans and
90% of the amounts disbursed for supporting services against statements of
expenditures (para 3.03 and Schedule A). The documentation for such expendi-
tures would not be submitted to the Bank for review but would be retained by
the FONDO and made available for inspection by the Barlk during the course of
supervision missions. The Bank would finance 100% of the foreign exchange
expenditure incurred for expert services and equipment and the beef marketing
study, and 100% of foreign expenditures or 90% of the total expenditures for
other technical services.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Special Development Fund
4.01      CB would channel the proceeds of the Bank loan through the Special
Development Fund (FONDO) to the banks eligible to participate, which would
include Banco Nacional (BN) and the six private commercial banks in the
country for on-lending to farmers. FONDO is empowered to support programs
aimed at increasing and diversifying the country's production and exports,
by making loans available to producers through private and Government-owned
institutions in accordance with regulations to be specified for each program.
4.02      FONDO has not yet commenced operations, but has negotiated two
loans from IDB for financing agro-industries and urban reconstruction,
through commercial banks and has finalized the relevant regulations and
procedures. FONDO's own capital would consist of (a) Government contribu-
tions to specific programs from time to time; (b) an unspecified portion of
CB's profits; (c) income from its own operations; and (d) donations. It
may use these resources as well as any funds obtained by way of medium- and
long-term loans from international institutions or through loans from other
sources or bond issues abroad or through internal flotation of bonds or bor-
rowings. In connection with the proposed project, FONDO would require re-
sources for (a) meeting its share of the project cost; (b) refinancing the



- 14 -
p:art.4cipating banks' loans before claiming reimbursement from the Bank; and
(c) meeting the costs of project administration (paras 4.03 and 4.04).  An
assurance was;, therefore, obtained that Governmtent would make available
a. sm of at least US$0.5 million equivalent to the FONDO as a condition of
.oaa effectiveness, and another amount of US$0.5 million equivalent at the
end of 12 months following that date.  FONDO's projected balance sheets and
profit and loss statements are shown in Annex 8.
Prore   Administration
4.03      CB has so far assigned, exclusively to the FONDO, only a business
management specialist who is designated as the DiLrector, and a livestock
speci.alist.  While remaining under the supervision of this Director, t he
organization needs strengthening in respect of technical and financial
project evaluation and assessment of investment effectiveiess. A quaJified
and experienced expert in agricultural and livestock credit a-id farm manags
ment would, therefore, be internationally recruited and appointed to serve
as the Technical Director of the project for a period of three years. Hie
would be responsible for training and supervising the technicians of the
banks and the FONDO, formulating procedures for appraisal and supervision
and assisting in coordination with the Ministry of Agriculture on pasture
seed multiplication and other technical services. CB would a'so appoint a
Nicaraguan Co-Director, who would be trained by, andi eventually take over
from, the Technical Director.  Assurances were obtained that a Technical
Director and a Nicaraguan Co-Director acceptable to the Bank, would be appoin-
ted on terms and conditions also to be satisfactory to the Bank and tiat a
Project Unit would be established in the CB. These appointments aind the
establishment of the Unit would be conditions of loan effective;r.ess.
4.04      CB would also appoint a crops specialist with experienee
in irrigated farming, and a project analyst.  BN has a technical staff of'
about 180 but the other banks have very few qualified techniciaus traftled
an a-ricultural project appraisal and supervision.  Project technicians would,
therefore, be appointed to work under the guidance of the Trecanica' Director
in assisting sub-borrowers to prepare their investment plans and to appraisc
and supervise the loan applications.  Their number would increase with loan
business and FONDO technicians would, in particular, serve banks whose ini-
tial busiLness would not suffice to pay for such staff,  Furtner, this staff
would be supported by the Latinocorisult livestock technicians under contract
with  B,  Assurances were obtained that, within three months of loan
effectiveness, CB would appoint a crops specialist, a project analyst
and two project technicians and that the participating banks would appoint,
between them, at least six technicians.
B:u co Nacional de Nicaragua
4.05      BeN is expected to play an important role in project lending, especial-
ly in financing the small beneficiar ies.  A Government-owned development bank
for agriculture and industry, which also undertakes normal commercial banking
functions, BN had capital and reserves, at the end of 1972, amounting to US$28.2
-llllion equivalent and deposits totaling US$44.4 mnillion equivalent.  External
borrowings amounted to US$65 million equivalent and its loan portfolio totaled



- 15 -
US$112 million equivalent as of the end of 1972, of which about 60% consisted
of medium- and long-term loans.  BN also expects to receive US$11 million,
for financing livestock development, from the proceeds of a loan made to the
Republic of Nicaragua by a consortium of British banks. T'.N proposes to use
these funds for making loans of US$100,000 or more for acquisition and devel-
opment of large ranches of 500 ha or more.
4.06      Poor performance in loan:recovery has been a weakness of BN's
credit operations. Arrears arose partly from crop failure during droughts
which periodically affect the country's predominantly rainfed agriculture
and partly from the bank's failure to observe sound principles of lending
and supervision. About US$38.5 million equivalent, or 34% of its total
loan portfolio, was considered frozen at the end of 1969, including loans
overdue and rescheduled and loans under legal action.  Of this total, the
share of agriculture, livestock, and rural credit was about 75%. Recog-
nizing the seriousness of the problem, IDB stipulated certain.related con-
ditions in connection with its loans (218/SF-NI and 177/OC-NI) foic agri-
cultural credit and livestock made in 1969. Principally, IDB required BN
to (a) engage a firm of consultants to analyze its loan portfolio; (b) ensure
a recovery of at least 95% of amounts falling due each year; (c) build up a
provision of at least 1% towards the bad debt reserve; and (d) bring down
the proportion of the frozen portfolio progressively between 1971 and 1973.
These requirements were subsequently modified by IDB when the severe drought
in 1971/72 threatened to affect loan recoveries.
4.07      Some improvement has resulted from BN's efforts to comply with
these conditions (Annex 9).  The volume of the frozen portfolio has been
reduced from US$38.5 million equivalent in 1969 to US$24.3 million (by 1972)
and, though the bad debt reserve has not been built up from profits, BN
wrote off about US$1.0 million of irrecoverable debts between 1970 and 1972,
4.08      Despite the difficulties faced in improving the working of BN, it
is desirable to secure its participation in the project in view of its wide
network of branches, its sizable complement of technical staff and its ex-
perience in financing small farmers. At the same time, it should be ensured,
in coordination with IDB, that BN continues its on-going efforts to improve
its financial position. Assurances were, therefore, obtained that (a) BN
would complete, within one year of the date of the Loan Agreement, investigation
of its agricultural loans overdue for more than three years on that date and
arrange for writing off irrecoverable amounts within a succeeding period of two
years and that Government would provide funds to BN for that purpose if BN can-
not fulfill this condition from its own resources; (b) Government would compen-
sate BN, within six months of the end of each fiscal year for net expenses
incurred by BN, on activities undertaken by it for development purposes; (c)
BN would contribute annually from its profits to a bad debt reserve so as to
build it up to 2% of its loan portfolio within two years of the date of the
Loan Agreement and maintain it at that level thereafter; and (d) BN would
undertake a study of its costs and expenses and adopt, within one year of the
date of the Loan Agreement, appropriate policies to increase its profits
which are at present negligible, affect its image as a borrower, and restrict
its ability to supervise loans and to build up reserves.

LenQiaR Policies and Procedures
4.09      Project technicians would assist applicants in the preparation of
farm plans and appraise sub-projects. Participating banks would make their
3Wln evaluation of the creditworthiness of individual applicants, would
approve sub-projects on the basis of project appraisal by their technicians,
and bear the full credit risk. Sub-loans to be refinanced by the FONDO
would ordinarily require approval by the Technical Director but he may delegate
such authority, at his discretion, to the Co-Director. No loan would be made
which by itself or together with an earlier sub-loan to the same sub-borrower,
exceeds US$100000 equivalent.
4i *O     Sub-loan applicants would be required to meet the creditworthiness
criteria and collateral requirements of the participating banks for medium-
and long-term agricultural loans. Security would normally include real
estate mortgages on titled land.  Even among those who may have earlier lacked
titles, many should have benefitted from IAN's on-going Land titling program
and several others have municipal titles which are acceptable to banks.
How,ever, as titling is a slow process and could hamper small farmiers, assurances
were obtained from Government that I.AN would expedite req-uests from borrowers
who have filed an application with IAN to obtain full title.  Chattel Tnortgage
on livestock and equipment would also be accepted.
4.11      Cash flows of investments proposed under the project as indicated
in the farm models (Annex 10) show that total repayment periods not exceeding
12 years (including grace periods up to four years) would be appropriate for
sub-loans for different types of investment.  The grace periods and periods
of repayment shown in Schedule A would not be exceeded without Bank's prior
approval and participating banks would provide the working capital require ents
of the project sub-borrowers.
Interest Rates
4.12      On term loans for agricultural purposes, banks in Nicaragua usually
charge a rate of 12% plus 1% commission plus, in some cases, 1% for techitical
assistance; and higher rates are often charged by private finance companies.
However, the excessive liquidity of the banks seems to have pusUed the rates
downward recently. Rates of 10% plus 1% are charged only in a few instances
such as BN's Credito Rural loans for small farmers and INFONAC's dairy loans.
While the annual rate of inflation in Nicaragua is about 2.5%, the banks pay
abQout8 to 9% on one- to three-year d  e o_it-s--and-pviy_g;stmer-eoaanies
pay 10.5% on five-year depoiits.  Costs of lpan dmani_st_at-ian.aehioh and-i
the eiperieuce of BN Y.iid INFONAC show's tat tney could not earn reasonable
profits mainly because their lending rates were too low to cover these and
other costs. Not only would the FONDO have to undertake promotional expenses
and help some banks with technical assistance but the commercial banks' margins
should suffice to cover a provision for bad debts and the cost of technical
staff whowm they have to recruit and train in advance of the build-up of this
portfolio. On the other hand, the expected returns on the project invest-
mients are adequate to bear a rate of up to 12%. Lower lending rates and larger
margins to participating banks would be justified only for stimulating lending
to small beneficiaries.  The banks' cost of making loans to these sub-borrowers

4.13      The lending terms and conditions are set out in detail in Schedule A.

Accounting and Audit
4.14      The auditing and accounting procedures are generally satisfactory
(Annex 9). Assurances were obtained that separate accounts would be main-
tained for the project at the FONDO and at each of the participating banks
showing separately the loans made to small beneficiaries and to others and
that the FONDO would supply to the Bank periodical progress reports on project
operations. The FONDO's accounts would be audited by an auditor who is ap-
pointed, under the statute of the CB, by the Board of the CB and who is inde-
pendent of the control of the President of the CB. The participating banks'
accounts are audited by independent auditing firms of standing. Assurances
were obtained that the accounts of the CB, the FONDO and the participating
banks would be audited by auditors acceptable to the Bank and that the audited
statements of accounts and auditors' reports would be sent to the Bank within
four months of the end of the various institutions' fiscal years.
V. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, AND PRODUCER BENEFITS
A. Production
5.01      The project would assist Nicaragua in increasing its output of beef
and milk. After about 10 years, project ranches and farms would supply an
additional 38,700 head for slaughter annually or an increase of about 10%
over present national offtake. The incremental production of beef would be
about 7,000 m tons 1/per year with a value of C$38.0 million (US$5.43
million) at present prices to producers, or C$63 million (US$9.0 million),
f.o.b., for US markets.
.        1/   Average liveweight of 360 kg, or 180 kg of beef (5.5 animals/ton).



.- 18 -
5.02      Incremental milk production would be about 15.5 million liters
annually valued at current prices at about C$12.4 million (US$1.80 million).
The additional milk production from project farms would represent a 9.6%
increase over present production. In addition to increases in milk and beef
for consumption or export, approximately 7,600 surplus breeding animals would
be available from project ranches and farms for sale to the livestock
industry. These would be worth about C$6.8 million (about US$1 million) at
current prices.
5c03      Production from investment in irrigation is less easy to specify
as a variety of crops may well be involved (Annex 4). On the basis of the
illustrative farm models, additional annual production would amount to
about 130,080 tons of sugarcane and 7,000 tons of plantain, representinig 7%
and 8%, respectively, of current Nicaraguan production.
B. Marketing
5.04      Beef marketing is dominated by the operations of four exporting
slaughterhouses, which currently handle about 190,000 head per year, or 60%
of total cattle slaughterings. Most of the remainder is slaughtered Dy
licensed butchers in ill-equipped municipal slaughterhouses. Sales to
export abattoirs are generally made on a liveweight basis and there is need
to introduce quality differential payments. For this purpose, a marketing
study would be financed under the project (para 3.12).  Exports are made
almost exclusively to the US market and prices paid to producers reflect US
market price trends. Prospects on both external and local -markets are good.
Slaughterhouses operate under seasonally fluctuating supply ccnditions and
their existing capacity can handle the additional production expected to be
generated by the project, particularly as this will help to even out some
of the seasonal variability in supplies (Annex 3). There is need to improve
facilities for the marketing of live animals and the project contains provi-
sions for this purpose (para 3.13).
5.05      An estimated 88% of the milk produced in Nicaragua is sold either
as fluid milk or as cheese.  The remainder is handled by the pasteurizing
plants serving the Managuan market or producing dried milk for export to
CACM countries. Demand is increasing in both sectors, which should be able
to handle without difficulty the expected additional production due to the
project (Annex 3).
5.06      The projected annual increase in sugarcane production would be
concentrated in outgrowers' holdings serving existing sugar mills, where
there is sufficient excess processing capacity to handle it. In the case
of plantains, the estimated additional production would be less than the
current level of imports and existing internal marketing channels are capable
of handling the quantities involved (Annex 4). Where production of other
crops is proposed, sub-loan appraisals would have to show that satisfactory
outlets exist, whether on internal or external markets (para 3.09).



- 19 -

rates of return are considered satisfactory. In the case of livestock en-

VI. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
6.01      By financing on-farm investment, the project would not only increase
productivity and production on about 550 ranches/farms, but it would also have
a beneficial demonstration effect on the country's livestock industry, which
has been handicapped by the lack of extension services. Specifically, the
project would bring about improvement in animal husbandry practices, pasture
management and the general level of technology and supply of breeding stock.
By promoting specialization to replace the present practice of mixing beef



- 21 -
and dairy and introducing better management, the project would also help
raise technical coefficients of milk production such as the calving rate and
lactation rates.
6.02      Various features incorporated in the project would help ensure that
about 150 to 200 loans are made to relatively smaller investors and provide
the basis for a later and larger program for this sector.  The project would
also provide for fuller employment of family labor. Mechanization is envisaged
only where it is essential and manual labor would be employed for most opera-
tions such as land clearing and development. Ancillary services such as
transport and processing would further augment employment opportunities.
6.03      The impact of the project on institutional development for financing
on-farm investment would be significant. Private commercial banks would be
involved in this activity on a major scale for the first time an!J would build
up a cadre of technicians equipped to handle this business. The project would
help strengthen BN as an instrument of such credit, especially for small farm-
ers, by helping to bring about financial and organizational improvements in
its working. The Special Project Unit would provide, through its Technical
Director and other technicians, a source of expertise not only in credit but
generally in regard to improved production practices,
6.04      By increasing the production of beef, milk, and some irrigated crops,
the project would help feed a growing population and improve Nicaragua's bal-
ance of payments, both by sustaining or increasing exports (beef, milk pro-
ducts and sugar) and by promoting import substitution (plantains and vege-
tables). The incremental exports of beef, milk and sugar are estimated at
full development to earn US$9 million, US$2 million and US$0.8 million, re-
spectively. The impact of the project in terms of other irrigated crops is
less clear as the financing of several crops is permitted and the actual re-
sults would depend on the demand. In particular, Nicaragua, which is a net
importer of onions, plantains, and vegetables, would be able to meet its
domestic consumption requirements of these crops increasingly from local
production.
6.05      The economic rate of return of the project is estimated at 25%
(Annex 11).

Agricultural Background
General
1.        Forming part of the Central American isthmus between the Atlantic
and Pacific Oceans, Nicaragua is bordered on the north by Honduras and on
the south by Costa Rica. During the decade 1960-70 the rate of population
growth was put at 3.1%, and Nicaragua's total population in 1971 was estimated
at 1.9 million. But with a total land area of. 118,358 km2, the density of
population (16 per km2) is the lowest of the five Central American countries
(Table 1).
2.        In 1971, the area farmed (48,900 km2) accounted for only 40% of
the total land area. According to the 1963 agricultural census, the distri-
bution of farm land by agricultural use was as follows:
Area
000 ha      Percent
Arnual crops                              384          10.2
Permanent crops                           156           4.1
Fallow                                    333           8.8
Pastures                                1,749         46.4
Other                                   1,146         30.5
Total Farm Area:                  3,768         100.0
Land use statistics for 1971 are currently being processed but the only
significant changes these are likely to show is a further increase in the
share of pastures, reflecting the growth of extensive livestock operations,
and a 30% expansion in the total area farmed (para 4 and Table 2).
3.        The three distinct zones to be found in Nicaragua are as follows:
(a)  Pacific Zone.  This consists of the Pacific coastal plains and
hills and the adjoining Rift Valley, with its two lakes (Nicaragua
and Managua) and line of active volcanoes. Rainfall varies from
2,500 mm in the north to 1,500 mm in the south.  With 60% of the
total population and a density of 62.4 per km2, the Pacific Zone
is the most developed region of Nicaragua.  Its fertile volcanic
0f                     soils account for the bulk of the production of cotton, sugar-
cane, bananas, and irrigated rice;



ANNEX I
Page 2
(b)  Central Zone.  Embracing the mountainous regions of the center
and north, with lower annual rainfall (500 to 2,000 mm) and
generally poorer lateritic-type soils, interspersed with more
fertile alluvial soils in valley bottoms, this zone contains
some 32% of the total population, with a density of 18 per km2.
Much of Nicaragua's coffee and livestock production is in this
zone; and
(c)  Atlantic Zone.  With a high rainfall (2,500 mm to 6,000 mm) and
relatively poor soils, mostly of lateritic origin, but leached
and deficient in both phosphorus and potassium, the Atlantic
plain is covered largely by tropical rain forest. At the
roment, this zone is largely underdeveloped and contains less
than 9% of the total population, with an average density of 2
per km2. As roads are built and settlement increases, it should
be possible to tap the potential of this zone for livestock and
forestry production.
4.        The expansion of land farmed by zone over the last 20 years is
shown below:
1952                  1963                    1971
Zone                             '000 ha       7 '000 ha h
Pacific        915      39        1,258         33      1,446          30     
Central      1,212      52       1,987          53      2,343         48
Atlantic       211       9          522         14      1 080         22
Total  2 338    100         3,767        100       4,869        100
These figures show that the rate of expansion between 1952 and 1971 varied
considerably between zones, reflecting in particular the openiing of land -in
the Atlantic Zone.
Agriculture in the Economy
5.        Agriculture remains the most important sector in the Nicaraguan
economy, although its contribution is slowly declining, as shown by the
following table and Table 3:
Share of
Agriculture                     1950       1960       1970
(a)  of GDP                            31        27         25
(b)  of economically active
population                    67         60         53
(c)  of exports (by value)             72        78         69



ANNEX 1
Page 3
Agriculture has retained its position in the economy because production for
internal consumption has generally kept pace with the growth of population,
while production fot export has both grown and been diversified. In 1950,
coffee accounted fo:r 67% of agricultural exports, whereas by 1971 the major
components of agricultural exports were cotton (31%), coffee (22%), meat (22%),
and sugar (9%). Agricultural exports for the three years 1969-71 represented
50% of total exports, and the favorable agricultural balance of trade for the
same period averaged over US$70 million.
Agricultural Production
6.        Crops.  The area and production of Nicaragua's main crops in 1971
were as follows:
Area      Estimated Percentage of          Production
Crops   ('000 ha)    Total Cultivated Area          ('000 m tons)
Corn         255               37                     232.9
Coffee        84               15                      43.6/1
Cotton       94                13                     237.0/2
Beans         61                8                      59.4
Sorghum       56                7                      55.6
Sugarcane    32                 4                   1,802.4
Rice          25                3                      49.8/3
/1   Green beans.
/2   Seed cotton.
7T   Milled rice.
7.        The steady rise since 1951 in the output of maize (74%) and beans
(54%), together with the doubling of the level of rice output since 1963,
largely due to the expansion of the area under irrigation, has enabled food
crop supplies to keep pace with the growth in consumption occasioned by in-
creases in population and living standards (Table 4). As regards export crops,
cotton output has varied with world market price changes but in 1971 was more
than five times greater than in 1951, while, over the same period, coffee and
sugarcane production more than doubled. Nicaragua has thus successfully in-
creased and diversified its participation in world trade in agricultural
products (Table 5).



ANNEX 1
Page 4
8.        Of the total estimated cultivated area of 700,000 ha, only 41,000 ha
(or less than 6%) are under irrigation, despite the fact that, particularly
in the Pacific and Central Zones, there is considerable annual variation in
the amounts and distribution of rainfall. The reasons for the relatively
slow development of irrigation include (a) the lack of pressure on land, (b)
the relatively high costs and other difficulties encountered in some irriga-
tion development schemes, (c) limited availability of assured water resources,
and (d) constraints (lack of research and production and marketing problems)
to the development of suitable crops, particularly in the cotton area (Annex 4).
9.        Livestock.  The livestock sector, currently contributing about 9%
of GDP, has shown a high rate of growth over the last decade.  The absolute
value of livestock production rose from C$197 million in 1960 to C$704 million
in 1971. Production of beef and milk accounted for nearly 80% of total live-
stock production by value in 1971. Under the influence of rising world market
prices, beef production showed the highest rate of growth (21.5% for the
period 1965-71). While domestic per capita consumption was maintained at
around 11 kg per year, the value of beef exports (mostly to the USA) increased
nearly tenfold between 1960 and 1971, when they constituted 22% of total
agricultural exports (Table 3). This development came largely from expansion
of extensive livestock production as reflected in the growth of pastures, which,
at present, account for about 50% of total farm land. In the future, however,
more emphasis should be placed on the adoption of improved husbandry techni-
ques and the achievement of higher technical coefficients (Annex 2).
Land Tenure
10.       Distribution of land holdings is uneven.  About one-half of all
farms are less than 7 ha in size and cover only 3.5% of the total farm area,
while, at the other extreme, the largest farms, each over 350 ha in size,
represent only 1.5% of the total number but account for 41% of the farm area
(Table 6). Moreover, land under use titles and land occupied without legal
title by squatters represented 30% of the total farmed area according to the
1963 census.
11.       Instead of adopting any land redistribution measures, Nicaragua has
chosen to encourage settlement in the Atlantic Zone and accelerate the issue
of land titles. Such work has been undertaken by the Agrarian Institute of
Nicaragua (instituto Agrario de Nicaragua - IAN), which was established under
the Agrarian Reform Law of 1963, but its impact has been limited by its small
budget (C$10 million, or US$1.4 million) and the fact that only 20% of its
staff operates outside Managua. Up to the end of 1972, IAN's program of
issuing clear titles which guarantee security of land tenure has benefited
15,103 families in a total area of 913,217 ha, mainly in the North, Central,                 6
and Atlantic Zones.  !AN acquired 2.8 million ha to allot to landless farmers.
Under the colonization program, IAN established colonies for some of these
beneficiaries, granting them technical assistance for production, social,
and cultural activities. Up to 1973, the program has assisted 3,562 families



ANNEX 1
Page 5
living in 38 agricultural colonies and 11 settlements, covering a total area
of 12,443 ha in agriculture and 8,426 in livestock. The colonists receive
advice in preparing credit applications for Banco Nacional de Nicaragua (BN)
and in marketing of farm products, and financial assistance from the
Government for basic infrastructure works which comprise the community
development effort. Under its peasants' training program, IAN seeks to
improve the socio-economic conditions of rural families through several
courses on rural development and cooperatives. So far, 113 courses have been
given to more than 5,000 participating peasants, and 22 cooperatives have been
formed with 1,240 members. IAN also helps resolve land tenure disputes, avoid-
ing land encroachments and ejections. Under a special program for the develop-
ment of new areas, IAN is attempting to bring new land into production for
transfer to landless peasants. A notable project in this category is Rigoberto
Cabezas involving 345,000 ha. IDB assistance is being sought to carry it out.
Dual Structure of Agriculture
12.      Based on skewed land distribution, Nicaraguan agriculture has a
markedly dualistic structure.  On the one hand, the medium- and large-size
farmers, particularly in the Pacific Zone, constitute a modern sector, which,
utilizing most of the available credit, fertilizers, and machinery, is
responsible for the production of the major export crops (cotton, coffee,
sugarcane, and bananas) as well as livestock and irrigated rice. On the
other hand, the traditional sector, consisting broadly of the farms of less
than 7 ha in size (50% of the total number), produces, under rainfed conditions,
the bulk of the corn, sorghum, beans, and upland rice. Relatively untouched
by extension services and credit, this sector has grown by an extension of the
area cultivated while yields have remained stagnant.
13.       The two sectors overlap in many areas and traditional-type farmers
supplement their incomes by working on cotton, sugarcane, and coffee farms,
particularly during the harvest season. Only in this way have they been able
to share in the general prosperity, the benefits of which have gone largely
to the larger farmer.
14.       In both modern and traditional farming sectors, and in livestock as
well as crop production, the relative availability of land has led to a
general reliance on extensive methods to increase production. Even in the
case of cotton, the fact that land can be leased on an annual basis permits
variation of the area planted in response to changes in world market prices
(Table 4).
Development Strategy
S
15.       The underlying objectives of agricultural development in Nicaragua
have been to maintain self-sufficiency in food production and to diversify
for export. The strategy adopted to attain these objectives reflects both
the dual structure outlined above and the prevailing political philosophy.
Instead of engaging in central planning, Government has placed its reliance
on providing the right climate in which the modern farm sector could operate
successfully. This sector is dominated by farmers with a strictly commercial



ANNrEX 1
Page 6
outlook and, in most cases, other business interests, who have shown themselves
quick to take advantage of any profitable opportunities. Nicaragua owes her
successful development of agricultural production and adaptability in meeting
changing demand on the world's markets to their enterprise.
16.       In accordance with this strategy, taxes on land and agricultural
enterprises are minimal, rates of duty on agricultural inputs (most of which
are imported) are low (5% to 7%), and, above all, bank credit has been a major
instrument in promoting production expansion in the modern farm sector (Annex 2).
17.       Measures to assist the traditional farming sector have been less
effective. Due to inadequate budgetary provision and insufficient coordina-
tion with marketing and credit, the research, extension, and education services
of the Ministry of Agriculture have had little impact. A rural credit program
has been operated by BN with mixed results, but for the most part, the small
farmer has little knowledge of improved techniques applicable to his level
of production and little access to institutional credit should he decide tc
introduce new methods.
Supporting Services
18.       As a result of this development strategy and the dual structure of
Nicaraguan agriculture, there is a varied pattern of supporting services. The
fact that the role of the Ministry of Agriculture is very limited has led
many other agencies to come into the field, and this and the absence of an              0
overall plan has resulted in considerable lack of coordination.
19.       Research, carried out by the Ministry of Agriculture at its three
stations, has dealt mainly with basic grains produced by the traditional farm
sector and has suffered from lack of adequate resources. For other crops,
research has mostly been on an ad hoc basis -- a notable exception being
cotton for which a comprehensive research station is operated by the
National Cotton Commission. Livestock research, including pasture experi-
mentation, has been conducted only on a sporadic basis (Annex 3). Because
of these weaknesses, little progress has been made in developir.g improved
breeds of livestock, particularly in view of the predominance of dual purpose
animals, and improved planting material, except in the case of cotton and
irrigated rice.
20.       The work of the official extension service of the Ministry of
Agriculture is handicapped by lack of resources (it has only 42 field staff)
and by insufficient coordination with research, credit and marketing, so that
its impact on the traditional farm sector has been small.  Where extension
has been associated with credit, as in the case of BN and Instituto de Fomento               0
Nacional (IN701,O7AC) programs, better results have been achieved (Annexes 2 and 3).



ANNEX 1
Page 7
21.       Agricultural training is available at both high school and university
levels. Degree courses in agriculture are conducted at the National School
of Agriculture but most post-graduate students go to foreign universities.
The output is less than 10 graduates and some 150 intermediate students per
year, which is insufficient to meet the keen demand for graduates in the
development agencies and the private sector.
22.       On the marketing side, the National Institute for External and
Internal Trade (Instituto Nacional de Comercio Externo y Interno - INCEI) was
formed with the basic objective of controlling the supply of basic grains.
Its efforts were first hampered by lack of storage facilities but this has
now been remedied by the erection of 100 Cantonal centers (Centros Agricolas
Cantonales) and two terminal storage depots under a BN program with Export-
Import Bank finance. INCEI's unhappy experiences in rice marketing, where its
support buying operations broke down in 1969 as a result of the price having
been fixed at too high a level (Annex 4), together with its inability to
compete with grain middlemen in promptness of payment, have led to loss of
confidence by small farmers and so restricted the role it can play.
23.       The total consumption of fertilizers grew from less than 10,000 m
tons in 1960 to nearly 57,000 m tons in 1971, while the use of insecticides
remained steady over the same period at about 12,000 tons. However, the modern
farm sector accounts for 90% of the use of these inputs, with cotton taking the
*SS"       major share (72%).  All the fertilizers and 25% of the insecticides are im-
ported and there are two fertilizer mixing plants.  The Pacific Zone partic-
ularly has a good network of distributors, and, in the main cotton area,
specialized contracting firms undertake aerial crop spraying.
24.       Mechanization shows a similar pattern.  Of an estimated total
tractor park of 3,577, nearly 50% are engaged in cotton production. All
tractors and most of their associated implements are imported; the current
agricultural tractor import rate is about 150 per year and there is a good
network of distributors and repair and spare part facilities.
Recent Developments
25.       With technical assistance from the Inter-American Institute of
Agricultural Sciences (Instituto Interamericano de Ciencias Agricolas) and
a grant from IDB, a National Commission has prepared proposals to strengthen
and expand the research, extension, and educational activities of the Ministry
of Agriculture over a five-year period, 1972-76. As a first step, the three
branches of the Ministry handling these activities were integrated as of
January 1973 but further action has been delayed because of the earthquake.
26.       Under the direction of the National Agricultural Committee, a
special Sector Analysis Unit (Unidad de Analisis Sectorial - UNASEC) has been
established in the Ministry of Agriculture, with technical and financial
assistance provided by USAID. During 1971 and 1972 UNASEC carried out a sample
agricultural census to update the results of the 1963 census and to provide



ANNEX 1
Page 8
data on which proposals for long-term agricultural development priorities
could be based. The incidence of the drought of 1972 and the Managua earth-
quake of December 23, 1972, however, required UNASEC to direct its attention
to preparation of an emergency plan to deal with the effects of these disasters
(para 29).
27.       From the agricultural point of view, the results of the drought
were disastrous while those of the earthquake have been negligible. From
virtual self-sufficiency in food production, UNASEC estimates that, following
the drought, there will be production deficits of the following order in the
1972/73 harvest:
Estimated Production Deficit
Percentage of
Crop                       mt              Estimated Consumption
Maize                      66,000                   30
Beans                       9,000                   20
Sorghum                   28,500                    40
and that there may well be reductions in the production of export crops,
notably cotton and coffee, with consequent losses of foreign exchange earn-
ings.
28.       Other consequences of the drought are reduced rural incomes, an
increase in rural unemployment, and financial difficulties and lack of working
capital, particularly for small producers. In addition to its disturbance
of commercial channels for the distribution of inputs and the marketing of
agricultural products, the earthquake has added to the general unemployment
problem.
29.       The emergency plan prepared by UNASEC proposes:
(a) integrated action by the Ministry of Agriculture, BN, INCEI,
and IAN, to provide inputs, short-term finance, and assured
but flexible market outlets to revive, in particular, the
production of maize, beans, and sorghum in the traditional
farm sector;
(b) concentration of commercial banking credit, with external
aid where possible, for export production, including livestock;
(c) a program for the construction of 713 km of new roads and
t;he maintenance of 205 km of existing roads to promote the
opening up of new production areas and to alleviate rural
unemployment; and
(d) emergency livestock schemes for pasture improvement, supple-
mentary feeding, feed lot operations, and pig development.



ANNEX 1
Page 9
30.       Apart from considerable additional crop loan requirements, it is
estimated that INCEI would need C$26.9 million (US$3.8 million) to finance its
proposed grain purchases and that the road construction and maintenance would
cost some C$204 million and give employment to about 4,000 people.
31.       Financial considerations may well dictate some scaling down of the
size of this emergency plan, but, even if only implemented in part, it could
well have important long-term implications by providing the foundation for
future coordinated institutional approaches to the problem of promoting the
development of the traditional farm sector.
32.       A temporary export duty of 10% is being imposed on agricultural
exports during 1973 and 1974 to help finance the costs of reconstruction.
The firmness of most world market prices has reduced the impact of this sur-
charge, which will have the incidental effect of making production for the
home market relatively more attractive.
Future Prospects
33.       Given Nicaragua's natural advantages in respect of soil, climate
and rainfall, and available land, together with its nearness to the US market,
there is general agreement that prospects for the further development of the
livestock sector are very favorable. The present position of that sector is
analyzed in detail in Annex 3.
34.       Until the on-going sector survey has been completed and firm con-
clusions as to specific development priorities can be formed, it will be
difficult to specify, in other than general terms, crops for which medium-
and long-term credit will be required. In the meantime, however, it is consid-
ered that the main demand for investment will be for the installation of
irrigation facilities. However, in addition to the constraints mentioned
in paragraph 8, the fact that investments have already been made in irrigation
for the production of such crops as sugarcane, bananas, tobacco, and rice is
likely to restrict the rate of expansion of irrigation in Nicaragua in the
immediate future. Annex 4 sets out the background for development of irriga-
tion in Nicaragua and details project proposals in this field.

NICARAGUA
AGRICTLTIURA  CREDIT PROJECT
The Banking SYstem and Agricultural Credit
A. Banking System
Introduction
1.        The Nicaraguan banking system consists of the Central Bank (CB),
a Government-omned commercial hank -- Banco Nacional de Nicaragua (BN), the
Instituto de Fomento Nacional (INFONAC), six private commercial banks, four
private finance companies, 10 insurance companies, a Government-owned housing
bank -- Banco de la Vivienda (By7) associated with a system of savings and
loans associations, four general storage companies, and a small loan bank
-- Banco de Credito Popular (BC?). The banking system is regulated by the
General Banking LaT- (1963), the organic laws of the Central Bank (1960), the
Banco Nacional de Nicaragua's organic laws (1940 and 1970), INFONAC (1953),
the National Systerm of Savings and Loans (1966), and other economic laws.
BN is the largest bank in the country and INFONAC operates as a Government
instrument to promote new activities contributing to economic development.
Total assets of these institutions at the end of September 1972 were as
follows:
