The Central Bank
2.        The Central Bank, founded in 1961, has legal control over the supply
of money and credit and is empowered to control discount rates, legal reserve
requirements of banks and other institutions, credit ceilings and portfolio
structure, interest rates, and level of foreign exchange deposits and credit,
and it is authorized to issue licenses for new banks or finance companies.
The Superintendent of Banks and Other Institutions has regulatory powers over



ANNEX 2
Page 2
administration procedures, credit management, and accounting and auditing standars
of the banks, finance companies, insurance companies, and storage companies.
3.        Organization.  The CB is administered by a six-member Board of
Directors, which is appointed by the President of the Republic and includes
(a) the Yinister of Economy, Industry, and Commerce; (b) the Yinister of the
Treasury and Public Credit; (c) a representative of the official banks and
institutions; (d) a representative of the private banks and finance companies;
(e) a representative of the Chambers and Associations of Agriculture, Industry
and Livestock Sector; and (f) a representative of the minority party. Each
Director has an alternate and the President of CB acts as Chairman of the Board.
The Board of Directors delegates executive authority to the President and to a
manager appointed bv the Board on his recommendation.
4.        The CB is authorized to set up certain funds to perform activities
beyond its role as the Central Bank. nne of these, the Fund for Production
Develonment (PDF), was formed in 1966 with the sale of livestock and industry
development bonds issued by CB, according to a Government mandate and operating
under rules laid down by the CB directorate. The first two series of the
bonds were bought bv the private banks and their proceeds were on-lent, about
half and half to TNFONAC and BN.  Because of the tight liquidity situation of
both the institutions, a third series of bonds was issued early in 1971 to
finance the repayment of the first two series.
5.        At year-end 1972, the unpaid balances on these bonds were US$4.48
million due from Banco Nacional de Nicaragua and US$5.24 million from INFONAC;
on the other hand, CB has paid the equivalent of US$2.20 million to the holders
of the bonds and is planning to redeem the bonds at the end of 1976.
6.        PDF was to be liquidated in early 1972 as a new fund (para. 7) was
being planned to take over its assets. However, this idea has now been aban-
doned and the CB now has two funds to perform similar activities.
7.        Special Development Fund.  Under the orovision of the organic law
and by presidential decree, CB has been authorized to administer, on behalf of
the Government, a Special Development Ftnd (FONDO) for financing programs through
the commercial banks and other lending institutions. FONDO will be the channel
through w^hlch the funds will be routed for two TDB projects for financing con-
struction companies, building materials, manufacturing and agro-industries.
FONDO is discussed in detail in Annex 9.
8,.       Functions.  CB sets regulatory measures for the entire banking
systen.  Silnce the econonv of Nicaragua is open and CB does not offer discounting
facilities as yet. the banks tend to use a number of foreign sources of credit.
CB has, therefore, set limits of external borrowing for the private banks and
especialiy for BN and INFONAC.  It also regulates the expansion of lending and
borrowin- onerations and has limited the annual rate of increase in portfolio
as follows:

scrutinized by the Superintendent of Banks.
Growth of the Banking System
12.       The growth of the banking system in Nicaragua has been closely
related to economic growth as a wqhole and, therefore, presents cyclical
fluctuations due to vagaries of the weather and drastic changes in the world
market for primary product exports.  Total deposits in the commercial banks
grew at variable rates which ranged from 1.6% to 32.6% per year during the
1964-68 period.  In 1972, the estimated rate of growth went up to 39.47.  The
volume of total deposits at year-end 1972 was US$105.9 million for BN,
US$112.3 million for the private commercial banks, and 1'S$4.7 million for
INFONAC.
13.       The entire banking system in Nicaragua has been using the interna-
tional money markets as a source of ftinds to help support their operations in
the country. Such external borrowings started in 1964 for the commercial banks
at a level of US$6.4 million and rose to a 1972 figure of US564.3 million.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
14.       Loans have grown very unsteadily, reaching a record high annual
rate of increase of 42.4%7 in 1965, dropDing dowm to 6.2% in 1970, and then
rising sharply to 30.4% in 1972. Loans overdue with the commercial banks have
been more or less stable in absolute terms in the last four years (around
US$7.0 million) but in relative terms they came down from 20% of the portfolio
in 1969 to an estimated 8.9% in 1972.
15.       The growth of the total assets of the banking system has somewhat
reflected the variations in performance of the agricuiltural sector in the
countrv; from a record low rate of 1.6% in 1968, assets grew at the rate of
39.9% in 1965; growth subsided to 4.8% in 1969 but again picked up in 1972
with 31%.
16.       The number of bank offices established has been related to the
expansion of assets and deposits. Commercial banks have developed a consider-
able branch network, especially in the urban areas, having, as of the end of
1972, before the eartht,uake, 18 branches in Managua and 28 branches in the
rest of the country. Banco Nacional, on the other hand, has 21 branches, of
which 15 are outside M1anagua, mainly in rural areas, plus 10 agencies and 22
rural credit agencies distributed all over the country. INFONAC and the
finance companies so far have no branches outside Yanagua.
Scope of Lending
17.       The banking system in Nicaragua makes loans for financing commerce,
agriculture, industry, and small enterprises. In addition, there is an
important component of credit to the export/imoort sector as well as for
consumption. Agriculture has accounted for a high, though variable, share
of total banIX credit over the last 10 years although it is difficult to assess
the real volume disbursed every year since the available data are gross of
delinquencies and rescheduling. However, as of December 31, 1972, of the
total outstanding loans for the entire banking system, including ImFONAC,
finance companies, and commercial banks, estimated at US$248.7 million,
US$108.9 million was for agriculture and livestock.
18.       Bank loans in Nicaragua are usually granted for short-term (up to
18 months)  -1 m                 -term  (un to five years) although Banco
Nacional and INFONAC have granted loans for longer periods under special
programs financed bjy external souirces of funds. In accordance with the FONDO
la,7, the CB can authorize any commercial hank to make loans for up to 10
years or more, provideA they are granted under programs financed with the
FONDO.
Reservc!s an-3 Interest Pates
1i'.      Commercial banks are required to keen with the CB legal reserves
on their deposits.  For private banks and  N,, the reserves should be at least
28% on their current denosits (27 for BN), 107 on their savings, and 10% on
their time deposits. On savings, there is a requirement of 5% for Savings



ANNEX 2
Page 5
and Loan Associations and 10% for INFONAC. The finance companies must keep
leaal reserves accordina to the maturity of their obligations:  10% for
deposits of less than a year, 6% for those between one and two years, 3% for
those between two- and three-year periods; and more for those with longer
maturity.
20.       Interest paid on deposits varies according to type of deposit and
institution. CB pays 8.5% on the Production Development Fund bonds while the
commercial banks are authorized to pay no more than 6.57 on savings and up to
9.5% on time deposits of three years or more. INFONAC is permitted to pay a
higher interest on deposits with maturity of more than thrmp v>o-c-         _    _
three to four years, 10.25% for 4 to 5 years, and 10.50% for more than 5 years.
Savings and loans associations are authorized to pay 7% on savings and from 8%
to 10.50% on bonds, while finance companies pay between 6.5% for deposits for
two to three months and up to 10.50% for deposits of five years or more.
21.       Interest rates charged on lending operations also vary, different
rates having been authorized by CB for loans to different sectors (Table 2).
Besides these authorized rates, most commercial banks charge commissions, which
range from 1/4 of 1% to 1%, while finance companies charge commission up to
4%. INFONAC has been authorized to charge as much commission as 3% on advances
out of an IDB loan.
B.  Agricultural Credit
22.       Institutional credit to the agricultural sector comes mainly from
BANCO Nacional and INFONAC. Although in recent years the private commercial
banks have also begun lending for agriculture, especially in export-oriented
crops and beef raislng and fattening, expansion of their activities is limited
by lack of adenuately located branches and the technical personnel to appraise
and supervise loans.
23.       Institutional credit is available only to a small percentage of
farmers and is mainly concentrated among large commercial producers. In part,
this has resulted from the lack of land titles on the part of some borrowers
but it appears to be due more to the lack of infrastructure and supporting
services, which affects production and repayment capacity. It is easier for
large farmers to get credit because they can obtain their own technical assist-
ance, are better located and have the facilities to market their production.
Other farmers mostly finance themselves or rely on an unorganized money market
of input supnliers, truckers, and processors, landlords, and relatives, who
make money or materials available at interest rates often above 10% per month.
24.       Of the total amount of institutional agricultural credit, the
highest pronortion is short term and intended for crop production, mainly
coffee, cotton, and irrigated rice. The number of livestock loans related to
beef growing and fattening has increased rapidly since the 1960's, but long-
term loans for beef breeding activities are more scarce.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
Interest Rates in Agriculture
25.       Interest rates charged to the farmers by the banking system are
related to the cost of money, the risk involved, CB regulations, and the
terms of lending. Short-term lending tends to be cheaper and some crops or
activities receive special treatment. In general, the private banks charge
11%, plus 1% commission, for short-term or production loans for crops such
as coffee, cotton, and sugarcane; 12%, plus 1% commission, for livestock
for beef production and other medium-term loans and '1%, plus 1% commission,
for beef fattening.
Collateral
26.       Most agricultural loans of banks in Nicaragua are secured by the
mortgage of land and chattel mortgage and, where land is not available, by
an urban mortgage or a co-signer. (An exception is that, under the Credito
Rural, BN accepts chattel mortgage as a substitute for mortgage of land).
Before IAN was established, there were many instances in which either no
titles to ownership existed or the available titles known as 'red' titles
were not acceptable to banks which considered them adequate only for securing
cultivation rights but not for collateral purposes. The IAN has, in the last
few years, issued clear titles for about 15,000 farms and, through this continu-
ing process, improved their acceptability to banks.  The participating banks
expect little difficulty in compliance with the collateral recuirement, on the
part of potential project sub-borrowers who, unlike marginal farmers, are not
likely to be handicapped in this respect. It is expected that, where necessary,
Government would help secure a clear title for such applicants through prornmpt
IAN action.
Cooperatives
27.       The cooperative organization in Nicaragua is legally supported by
a new General Law of Cooperatives (1971), which is under the jurisdiction of
the Department of Labor. This law is basically oriented towards labor co-
operatives and does not have specific provisions for agricultural cooperatives.
Since BN's organic law authorizes it to "promote, organize and finance" co-
operatives in which farmers can make efficient use of credit to obtain inputs
or services and market their production, it created a Division for Cooperatives
within its Department of Credit to encourage formation of such organizations
among its clients.  However, because of deficient legislation, corporations
sometimes call themselves cooperatives so that they can enjoy lower taxes
and other fiscal advantages, and this had led to confusion about the coopera-
tive movement and its principles.  CooDeratives, therefore, appear to have a
limited role in agricultural production, credit, and marketing, unless the
legal background is improved and Government gives them stronger support and
creates an atmosphere favorable to their working.
Loan Recoverv
28.       Loan delinquency has been rather high in agricultural credit, mainly
because of one or more of the following factors: (a) deficient supporting



ANNEX 2
Page 7
services: (b) inadequate land tenure system; tc) deficient marketing arrange-
ments; (d) poor loan appraisal and supervision; and (e) poor husbandry on the
farms. Agricultural lending has accounted for as high as 35% of frozen portfolio
in governmental institutions and up to 18% within the private banks.
International Finance for Agriculture and Livestock
29.       The banking system of Nicaragua has had access to external financ-
ing since 1965. The Bank was the first source of external funds for agricul-
tural credit with a loan of US$1.2 million in 1951 to BN for importing
machinery. Subsequently, IDB has made loans totaling US$23.7 million to
finance the Rural Credit Program, beef and dairy cattle, irrigated rice,
grains and other crops. USAID has also granted loans totaling US$16.0 million
to finance a supervised agricultural credit program, promotion of cooperatives,
and the recapitalization of Banco Nacional de Nicaragua.
30.       IDB has also granted loans to INFONAC totaling US$5.1 million to
finance dairy, beef, and pork production programs.  The disbursemeTt of the
last two loans, however, has been delayed because of INFONAC's failure to raise
counterpart funds and also because the list of goods and services did not corres-
pond to the farmers' demands. The Central American Bank has granted some
small loans to INFONAC for fisheries (US$0.76 million) and to BN for agro-
industries, as well as to a private enterprise for pork production; and many
foreign banks and manufacturing corporations have made short-term and medium-
term loans to the banking system for agriculture.
Banco Nacional de Nicaragua (BN)
31.       BN has been the most important source of institutional agricultural
credit in Nicaragua since 1924, when it was taken over by the federal Govern-
ment to act as a commercial bank and as a note-issuing institution. As the
oldest bank in the country, BN has developed a considerable network of branches
and rural agencies that facilitate its participation in agricultural and live-
stock credit projects. However, as a result of the Managua earthquake, BN's
main office and several urban branches were destroyed, and the bank was moved
temporarily to Masaya. Summarized financial accounts of BN are given in
Table 3.
32.       BN's operations are closely related to the industrial and agricul-
tural development of Nicaragua. Its development services to farmers on behalf
of the Government consist of project planning and execution and technical as-
sistance, and during the period 1969/71, these activities accounted for expend-
itures amounting to about C$6.2 millions per year (Annex 9).
33.       BN's lending program is divided into two parts:  (a) normal banking
credit for large farmers, and (b) the Rural Credit Program for small farmers.
The Rural Credit Program has as its objective the provision of assistance to
the bulk of the country's farmers who could not obtain credit from other
sources. Loans under this program now account for about 9% of BN's total
*        portfolio and about two-thirds of the number of loans outstanding.  The
average loan size is USS234.00.  The program also includes provision of a
reasonable amount of technical assistance -- 300 farmers per agent -- but, in
practice, extension has been ineffective.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
34.       About two-thirds of credit granted under this program has financed
annual rroduction costs, mainly for basic crops such as corn, beans, and
sorghum, while the remainder has been used for the purchase of cattle and
fa:m development works. During the 1971/72 production cycle, the program has
helped about 22,120 small and medium size farmers, wqith holdings averaging 2 ha.
35.       Taking into account the high cost of non-institutional credit for
this type of farming, the program seems to have benefited farmers and enabled
them to increase production. However, delinquency has been high, probably
because of the high proportion of short-term loans and the noor supporting
services.  Though increased output has apparently resulted from increasing
acreag,e, farmers have experienced difficulties in marketing surplus production
because of poor coordination between BN and INCEI.
36.       BN's agricultural portfolio has suffered from poor loan collections,
resulting from various factors such as periodical crop failure, inadequate super-
vision and lack of satisfactory arrangements for technical assistance and
marketing. BN has, however, taken several measures in the last few years for
improvement in this respect (Annex 9).
Instituto de Fomento Nacional (INFONAC)
37.       INFONAC, created by a Decree of 1952, was conceived as a governmen-
tal instrument to promote economic development in all the production sectors
of Nicaragua.  It combines the functions of bank, finance company, development
institute, research agency, and holding companv. Because of a lack of permanent
programs of financing, field offices, and technicians, and because of occasional
financial difficulties, INFONAC has plaved only a limited role in agricultural
lending but, even so, it has been more active than banking institutions other
than BN.
3f.       Organization and i-fanagement.  General nolicy and overall administra-
tion and credit policies are delegated by the Superior Council of the INFONAC
to the six-member Board of Directors, which is formed by its General Manager
(Chairman), a representative of the Presidency, twio representatives of the Su-
perior Council, a representative of the production sectors, and a representative
of the minority party.
30.       Day-to-day administration of the INFONAC is delegated to the General
MTanager, assisted by the deputy managers who head the departments.  INFONAC has
not yet established any branch outside lanagua, but instead has seconded several
technicians to *z.ork in the areas where snecific Trograms are executed.
4n,       Capital Structure.  INFONAC's summarized financial accounts for the
year 1970 to 1972 are showzn in Table 4.  INFONAC started with an authorized
capital of CS5n.O million (UTSS7.1 million) which has been paid over a 10-year
period.  In1er its law, INv0NAC is entitled to have its losses reimbursed by
the Government, as a cinital contriblution, but during the period 1969-71, such
contributions were not made available and the cumulative deficit at year-end
1971 was Uq,4.f  million.  However, in 1972, the Government made available
US$10.0 million that was used for repayment of external and domestic debts

and provision of counternart funds for IDB loans. On December 31, 1972, net
worth and reserves anounted to CS119.4 million (US$17.1 million).
41.       INFONAC's borrowings from the Central Bank are approved on a yearly
basis, mainly for short term; at year-end 1972, the outstanding balance with CB
was C$36.7 million (TUS$5.2 million).  Deposits and promissory notes amounted to
C$30.0 million (USA11.4 million); and external borrowings amounted to C$160.8
million (US$23.0 million) (Table 4).
42.       Operations.  INFONAC has been active in three types of
operations:  lending, equity contribution, and feasibility studies and
develonment of new industries.  As of December 1972,  INFONAC had US$8.3
million invested in shares of 19 companies covering various fields such as
steel mills, boatyards and boatlines, urban development, hotels and tourism,
chemicals, textiles, and fisheries.
43.       In its lending for agriculture and livestock, INFONAC has organized
the formation of integrated projects, and has therefore had to deal with large
clients or with comDanies in which financing takes the form of either equity or
loans. Short-term lending in agriculture covered banana (17 loans), tobacco
(136 loans), yuca (5 loans), and others (13 loans); and a stocking program
for livestock (8 loans), feedstuffs and hygiene (1 loan), and milk production
(11 loans).  Medium-term lending included 170 loans for banana, irrigation,
coffee, equipment, and tobacco and 190 loans for cattle stocking, milk
production, bee-honey production, pork production, and land improvements
for ranches. In long-term lending, 149 loans were for irrigation, banana,
tobacco, coffee, rice, and farm machinery, while livestock has 167 loans for
livestock stocking, bee-honev production, milk production, and pork production.
In most cases, thie three types of loans have gone to the same clients.
44.       INFONAC has been operating at sizable losses each year since 1965,
attributable to: its work on behalf of Government as a development institu-
tion;  its small ecuitv base for its large operations; a stable or gradually
declining interest yield; and increasing costs of borrowing.
45.       Income from lending operations is still lower if non-yielding port-
folio is taken into account. Interest actually collected represents only 45%
(estimated) of the interest accrued.  On the other hand, lending rates appear
to be unreal, especially in earlier years when a fairly large percentage of the
-portfol{o was made up of long-term, low interest loans.
46.       On December 31, 1972, TNFONAC had total ouitstanding liabilities of
USA41.25 million, about 14% hiTher than the book value of its net loan
portfolio.  About USS23.0 million, or 56% of its liabilities, were to foreign
lenders, about one-half to international agencies and one-half to private
foreign banks as loans and lines of credit. Annual debt service on the
foreign loans (excluding the lines of credit) is about US$6.0 million, or
US$7.0 million if allowance is made for reasonable retirement of the credit
*           lines.

Private Commercial Banks
47.       The six nrivately owned cornercial banks -- Banco de America, Banco
Nicaraguense, Banco Calley-Dagnall, Banco de Londres v Montreal, Bank of Ameri-
ca, and First National City Bank -- have so far made only a modest contribution
to agricultural credit.  HIowever, their role in this field can be improved if
there are incentives and viable projects. The restriction under the Banking Law
which rules out loans beyond five years ceases to apply in relation to conmercial
bank operations financed through the FONDO.

0                                           NICARAGUA
AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Livestock Development
A. Livestock Sector
Importance to Economy
1.          The absolute value of livestock production rose from C$197 million
in 1960 to C$625 million in 1970. In 1971 the value of production was esti-
mated at C$704 million while the export value of beef and milk in 1971 was
estimated at C$36.0 million, representing about 15% of total export values.
Firm and rising prices for beef will make livestock exports increasingly
important to the economy of Nicaragua.
Beef and Dairy-Cattle
2.          According to preliminary data from a sample survey undertaken in
1972 by the Sector Planning Unit under the Vice Minister for Agriculture, there
are about 2.6 million cattle in Nicaragua. Because many herds are dual-purpose
(beef and milk), the national herd cannot be subdivided on a functional basis
(Table 1) but specialization which is gradually taking place should make such
subdivision possible in the future. Stratification of beef production into
breeding, growing, and fattening zones or areas is also taking place, which
should lead to more efficient utilization of savannah, humid tropical, and
dry areas with access to crop by-products and sources of water for irrigated
pastures.
3.          Apart from a few purebred herds of Brahman, Santa Gertrudis, and
Brown Swiss, the commercial cattle are an admixture of Criollo (native cattle)
and introduced breeds, mainly Brahman and Brown Swiss. In some areas, there
is evidence of the introduction of the blood of Holstein, Jersey, and even
Hereford.  Future breeding policies will depend on the local climate and the
future specialization of the herd. Brahman would be initially most appropriate
for beef, Brown Swiss for dual or single purpose beef/milk herds, and Jersey
for purely milk herds.
4.          Generally, husbandry and production practices are poor.  The system
lacks direction and management and a technical basis for improving efficiency.
There are some efficiently run herds, however, which reflect possible improve-
ments. Nutrition is poor, good bulls are lacking, there is insufficient con-
trol of parasites and animal health, handling facilities are defective or defi-
cient, and calf care is poor. Mating is generally uncontrolled, selection for
improvement is insufficient and farm records are at a minimum. All these fac-
tors result in low production coefficients.
.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
5.          In the Central Zone, cattle production is extensive;  most lactating
cows continue to be milked, and steers are three to four years old before they
are ready for slaughter. This zone, however, is particularly important as the
main source of weaner steers for fattening on the more specialized farms of the
Pacific Zone, and hence further expansion of the industry depends on increased
offtake from these herds.
6.          Despite the excellent long-term potential for extensive ranching
development in the Pine Savanna region of the Atlantic zone, this region at
present contributes only a small proportion of the country's livestock pro-
duction.  There is, however, evidence of a small but progressive expansion of
cattle breeding in the newly colonized areas adjacent to the road leading to
Rama, and that development is expected to continue.
7.          Many specialized dairy and fattening units are concentrated in the
Pacific Zone where crop by-products and residues provide readily available
supplements and where, particularly for milk, there are attractive market pos-
sibilities: the more intensive fattening and dairy farms in the Pacific Zone
depend heavily on irrigation.
8.          The principal beef statistics available are shown in Table 20  The
composition of the national herd is approximately 39 to 40% females (1,000,000),
with an average weaning rate of about 45%, or an annual production of 450,000
new calves per year.  Adult mortality is about 5% (about 80,000) so that with
an annual extraction rate of over 13% per annum (345,000 head) there is a net
annual national gain of only about 25,000 (450,000 less 80,000 deaths less
345,000 sales), or slightly less than 17 per annum. This leaves very little
room for additional internal consumption or export without cutting into capi-
tal stocks. Technical and economically viable development is therefore an
urgent necessity.
Production Trends
9.          Offtake (including exports on the hoof) has risen from about 150,000
to 310,000 head per year between 1960 and 1970, with numbers slaughtered within
Nicaragua increasing as follows:
Item           1960           1970            1971
tlales         101,484 (75%)  205,061 (66%)   N.A.
Females         32 166 (25%)  104,964 (34%)   N.A.
Total          133,650         3102025       325,000
iO.         Production of milk, for which statistics may not be very reliable
since a large -proportion is consumed on the farm or marketed locally as cheese,
has risen from 60 million to 100 million liters per year in the same period.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
The number of pigs slaughtered annually almost doubled from 147,000 in 1960
to 286,000 in 1970 and decreased to 283,000 in 1972. Even more spectacular,
however, were the increases in poultry production, with chicken meat reported-
ly rising from 350 to 3,000 tons annually and egg production increasing by
over 400% to a total of 22 million dozen in 1972.
11.         According to an estimate made in March 1971, the proportion of cows
being milked decreased from 92% in 1960 to 80% in 1970.  The decline continues.
Calving percentages improved from 42% in 1960 to 47.5% in 1970, while dairy
milk production per cow increased from 1.8 liters/day to 2.3 liters during
the same period. Of total milk production, 3% represents wastes and losses
and 15% was used as animal feed (data were obtained from the Coordinated Milk
Office in Managua). Table 3 shows the population of cows and the volume of
milk production derived from the national herd, including beef cattle. Al-
though much of this production is consumed in fresh form or as cheese, a
small proportion is processed or pasteurized;  Nicaragua exported to El Sal-
vador and Guatemala over 1,300 tons of dried milk in 1970 and 1,430 tons in
1971.
12.         Beef production can be expected to maintain its dominant position
in the livestock industry even though Nicaragua cannot maintain the remarkable
rate of increase in herd offtake achieved during the last decade. These in-
creases in annual offtake have resulted largely from (a) intensification of
beef finishing operations resulting in a decrease in the age of steer slaugh-
ter;  and (b) improvements in markets and communications allowing the slaugh-
ter of old cows previously not marketed.
13.         While technical services to farmers have probably helped reduce
finishing ages for steers, their impact on weaning rates has been small. The
improved nutritional standards introduced by these services, together with
price incentives, have also contributed to an increase in average carcass
weight, boneless meat production per carcass rising from 289 lb to 321 lb
during the period 1960/72.
14.         Farmers are becoming receptive to advice, but the proportion of the
industry that has adopted new technology remains limited and overall livestock
productivity is low. On the semi-extensive breeding ranches in the Central
upland zone, weaning rates are about 40 to 45%; adult mortality, 5% per annum;
and liveweight gain, 250 gr per day. Lactating cows are usually milked and pro-
duce 1 to 2 liters per day.  Stocking rates are under one animal unit (AU) per
hectare.  Much of the low productivity is attributable to the nutritional status,
pa:rticularly in the long dry season, of the pastures in which Jaragua (Hypar-
rhenia rufa) is dominant.
15.         As a result of a package of improvements including routine pregnancy
diagnosis, seasonal mating, parasite control, pasture improvement and fencing
to permit rotational grazing, selected beef breeders have been able to raise
weaning rates to 70 to 80% and stocking rates to 2 AU per hectare. Similarly,
a number of specialized dairy farms serving the Managua market attain average



ANNEX 3
Page 4
yields of over 2,000 liters per lactation, Possibilities of utilizing crop
by-products, including molasses, cotton husk, and cotton seed meal and of
fodder conservation have also been partially explored on intensive fattening
and breeding farms on the Pacific coast.  The available examples of success-
fully applied new technology demonstrate the enormous technical potential for
improvement, particularly by raising nutritional standards through grass and
legume pasture establishment and mineral supplementation.
Animal Health
16.         Nicaragua is free from foot-and-mouth disease and disease ire-idenee
appears to be low. Attempts are being made to eradicate brucellosis. and pro                  -
gressive farmers generally immunize cattle against blackleg anLd, iEM certsi'r
areas, against anthrax.  Tick-borne anaplasmosis and piroplasmosis usual y af
fect imported temperate cattle.  The principal aniimal health hazard, hoa     v
result from external and internal parasites, so that regular dlpping and
drenching is necessary for optimum growth.  The most serious external pal asits
is the torsola fly, the high incidence of which leads to generally poor quaiity
hides. High calf mortality rates are attributable to poor nutritional levels
resulting from limited suckling of low yielding cows and the poor quaal:ty pa&
tures available to young weaned cattle.  Vampire bats constitute a nazard in
the hill areas northeast of Matagalpa and in parts of the Atlantic Zone wenhiclh
may be controlled in the future by systemic or topical blood decoagulants nov
being developed by a team working under the auspices of the US Governisent 
Agric_ltural Services
17.        Field Services.  There is no effective national agricuittra7  e;z-
tension service at present in Nicaragua.  The Ministry of Agriculture atz-ae,ts
to service the small farm sector through some 40 offices throughout the counvny
but it has too small a staff and insufficient facilities to mate any sigLifi-
cant development impact.  Technical services are provided by agencies suc i as
the Banco Nacional, INCEI, Prolacsa, and INFONAC, but such adivice is tie.c to
their lending or purchasing programs.  The most effective ad%risory service ii'
th is sector has been provided since 1966 by arn Argentinian consultancy flrm,
Latinoconsult, which has a contract with the Central Bank-, Lastinocosuli hLat
a 16-member team, including livestock technicians, veterinarians, and economists 
which pr ovides intensive advisory and management services to about 250 lar-ge
scale ranchers and intermittent advice to a further 380 livestock farmers. £h.e
group works closely with the Banco Nacional but its operatIo-ns are not tied to
the ban ks lending program.  In spite of impressive changes made on some irndivid-
ual farnms and the diffusion of practices to farnss outside the program, the na-
tional impact of Latinoconsult's work remains limited.
18.         TIe MiLnistry of Agriculture employs several veterinarians but they
are mainly concerned with inspection and control, particularly at abattoirs.
The service is currently expanding with French technical cooperation to provide
on-farm veterinary assistance to farmers in some regions. The Cattlemen's
Association is also recruiting veterinarians to serve its members,



ANNEX 3
Page 5
19.         Artificial Insemination.  Artificial insemination services, begun
in 1963, are operated by the National Guard in collaboration with a fUrm of
semen importers. The inseminators live on some 110 larger dairy and beef farms,
on which semen is cold-stored in dry ice flasks. In 1970, over 19,000 cows
were inseminated, of which about 70% were dairy cattle. Brown Swiss was the
most commonly used breed of dairy bull, while Brahman bulls were used in 85%
of the inseminations.
20.         Research.  Livestock research is conducted sporadically and lacks
objective and coordination. The Ministry of Agriculture does not carry out
any continuing program of investigation, but has served as collaborator in
several nutritional studies coordinated by INFONAC and the Banco Central and
carried out by staff of Louisiana and Florida State Universities. The very
limited pasture trials carried out by the Ministry and by individual farmers
have not produced any widely applicable results.
21.         Education.  Degree courses in agriculture are available at the
National School of Agriculture and a specialized Animal Husbandry course is
provided by the University of Central America. A number of agricultural
graduates have attended post-graduate courses at the Central American Business
Management Institute, but most post-graduate students study in the United
States. Since attractive employment opportunities exist for even partially
trained students, the annual out-turn of graduates is low relative to the
number enrolling:
No. of Students
Institution                          Enrolling        No. of Graduates
Agricultural High School (1969)          688                 162
National School of Agriculture           172                   7
(1970)
University of Central America            221                  15
(Livestock Faculty) (1970)
22.         All the institutions are short of full-time qualified staff. The
present state of agricultural research, extension, and teaching has been re-
viewed recently in an IDB-financed study, which identifies the investment and
organizational requirements for the proper servicing of the agricultural sec-
tor. The earthquake of 1972 has disrupted the training programs and the facili-
ties of the Livestock Faculty of the University have been destroyed. The
National School of Agriculture is now occupied by staff displaced from other
institutions.
23.         Government objectives are to encourage the beef and milk industries,
which contain as many as 50,000 producers, four export meat works, and four milk
plants but the prospects for such Government policy alone resulting in a modern
livestock sector are remote.  To realize these objectives, greater emphasis must



ANEX 3
Page 6
be placed on strengthening agricultural education and extension, animal health
services, secondary road development, and marketing services. Even if adequate
lines of credit are forthcoming, progress will at best be slow and the benefits
unevenly distributed, if supporting services are deficient.
B. Project Considerations
Strategy
24.         The strategy of the project would be to increase the output of
beef and milk in Nicaragua by raising the productivity of land, livestock,
and labor through the provision of long-term credit and technical services,
together with supporting services and working capital. Ranch/farm models pre-
sented in Annex 10 illustrate the type of investment necessary for achieving
the results envisaged from the project. Investments would be made in about
28,000 ha of improved pastures; over 1,000 corrals and farm workers' houses;
150 sets of machinery and equipment; 225 new irrigation units; 450 new live-
stock watering points; about 4,000 improved breeding animals; and about 1,000
km of new fences.
Production Units
25.       Rainfed Dairy Farm - 50 ha (Appendix 10-1).  Farms in this category
would be located in the Central Zone (Department of Boaco - Chontales). In
addition to providing investment capital for pasture, cattle, and improvement
of farm facilities, the project would emphasize controlled mating and selection
and animal health and parasite control. The improvement of pastures would ini-
tially require the importation of seeds of Panicaum Maximum, (enchous, Stoylo-
santhes, Centrosema, and other grasses and legumes adaptable to poor soils and
the variable rainfall pattern. The farm pattern would gradually be changed from
multi-purpose operations to single-purpose milk production, using dairy-type
bulls such as Brown Swiss or Jersey or semen.
26.         Production practices would be improved to achieve carrying capaci-
ties of 2 AU/ha, milk yields to 7 liters per cow/day, lactation periods to
280 days, and calving to 80%. Milk production should rise to 1,800 liters
per ha.
27,       Irri2ated Dairy Farms - 20 ha (Appendix 10-2). Selected small
farms now hardly providing a living family income would be transformed into
specialized units capable of supporting a growing family in improved circum-
stances.  The farms would be located in the Leon area of the Pacific Zone.
28.         Development would rely heavily on technical services to supervise
investments and the management of improved pastures under irrigation, milking
techniques, controlled mating, and culling. The herd would be improved by
introducing improved dairy-type cows and by the use of dairy-type bulls, such
as Brown Swiss or Jersey, and by artificial insemination.                              0



ANNEX 3
Page 7
29.         Resulting improvements would include an increase in carrying capa-
city from less than I AU/ha to 6 AU/ha, in calving from 55% to 80%, and in pro-
duction per cow/day from 4 to 8 liters, and extension of lactation by 100 days
and production per lactation from 720 to 2,000 liters. Milk production per ha
should go up from 650 liters to 6,500 liters at the end of the project.
30.       Rainfed Beef Breeding and Fattening Ranches - 300 ha LA2Lencix 10:),1
These ranches would be located mainly in the Central Zone. Great emphasis would
be placed on improving or replacing existing Jaragua pastures with appropriate
grass/legume introductions such as Panicum, Cenchons, Setaria, and other trovi-
cal grass species and Siratro, Centrosema, Desmodium, Dolilchus, Glyine, and
Soylosanthes legume species.  Inoculation and phosphate fertilizers would be
needed to initiate strong growth.  Parasite and disease control would be essen-
tial, together with seasonal calving and selection for cows of regualar breediikg
frequency. Beef breed bulls or semen would be used to improve the characteris-
tics of the now heterogeneousherds. Brahmans would be most favored to initially
improve beef qualities and resistance to local environmental conditŽons. M4ilk-
ing would. be phased out and working capital would be provided for the purchase
and fattening of steers.
31.         Benefits would be an increase in effective calving from 45% to 75%,
a reduction in adult mortality from 4% to 2%, a rise in carrinng capacity to
2 AU/ha, and an improvement of the extraction rate from 15% to 21% due to better
nutrition and a consequent lowering of age to turnoff.  The product4on of ha&
should rise from about 25 kg/ha to 80 kg/ha.
32,       Irrigated Pastures and Beef Fattening Farms - 20 ha (Apvi 
These farms would be generally in the Leon Department of the Pacif4.c Zo0'e.
Pasture management techniques employed here would be similar to those on irri-
gated dairy farms. After the sinking of the bore and purchase of irrigatiorn
equipment, the 20 ha would be subdivided into 20 paddocks, with a lane for
access to all fields. Cattle purchase would start after about four mbuths a&d
the area would then carry about 120 head.
33.         The type of steer to be bought would be half-cross Brahmin bo-ut ar.y
good quality steer would have to serve until breeding ranches built up their
output. All cattle entering would be weighed, vaccinated, and dosed and sprayed
for internal and external parasites. Average weights at purchase wouZd ?e
about 250 kg, and daily gains per head would be the order of up to 0/6 kgifday
over a fattening period of eight months.  Pastures would be densely stocked and
grazed in rotation about once a month. Annual fertilizer applicatior- would he
about 16 quintals per annum and mainly nitrogenous at first. Irrigation would
be spray type (36-hp motor for 420 gal/m)in  and water would be applied from
December to May at the rate of about 200 mm/month.
34.         Pasture carrying capacities would rise from 0.5 AU/ha to 6 AUTL/ha
and the incremental production/ha would be equivalent to 600 kg. Net fama- ia-
come would rise from virtually nothing to C$60,000 per annum after loan amnrni-
zation and the savings generated would suffice for all working capital needs.

39.          arketin  Centers for Cattle.  Two simple livestock marketing cen-
-ers would be constructed to provide decentralized services to cattlemen wish-
lng to sell or buy cattle.  The sale of live cattle is relatively unorganized,
in.efficient, and generally detrimental to the producer.  One large (and attrac-
tive) auction center, "SIJUBASTA" has beer. recently constructed and organized in



ANNEX 3
Page 9
Managua, but the outlying areas from which the cattle are drawn for sale remain
unserved. Investment would include simple cattle yards suitably subdivided,
a scale for weighing cattle, a simple sales ring for buyers and sellers, loading
and unloading ramps, water supplies, and office facilities. Funds for publicity
would also be required. These centers would be organized by the Cattlemen's
Association and supervised by personnel of the Ministry of Agriculture.
40.         These centers would help extend sales outlets at the sources of
production; ensure greater competition among cattle buyers and dealers, collect,
at published dates, large groups of steers for fattening females judged fit
for reproduction and slaughter cattle, and disseminate market price informa-
tion. It is expected that producers would receive higher prices through sale
on a known liveweight basis and in view of their better bargaining position
with intermediaries and dealers. The centers would also help compile various
livestock statistics and strengthen producer solidarity and cooperation.
41.         Artificial Insemination Center.  An artificial insemination center
would be established as a first stage in decentralization of such services now
concentrated in Managua. Investment items would include vehicles for insemina-
tors, a bulk liquid nitrogen tank and compressor, field tanks for transporting
semen, office equipment, and initial importation of semen ampoules. Organization
of this unit would be the responsibility of the Ministry of Agriculture.
C.  Marketing of Livestock Products
Beef Marketing
42.       Slaughterhouses.  The Nicaraguan beef exports rose from some US$3
million in 1960 to over US$26 million in 1970 (Tables 4 and 5) and these
spectacular increases have been accompanied by significant changes in marketing.
The slaughtering of cattle, previously carried out almost exclusively either in
small municipal abattoirs or on producers' farms is now dominated by the oper-
ations of four modern slaughterhouses, producing mainly for export (Table 6).
The location, date of establishment, and current handling capacities of the four
"export" meatworks are as follows:

43.         All plants are equipped for slaughtering and for breaking down
carcasses into frozen boneless cuts, which are packed for export in _60-lb
containers.  With the expansion of exports of meat  (para 42), the abattoirs
are also investing in equipment for the production and packaging of portion-
control cuts, hamburgers, and bologna.  Both Ifagan and Igosa have large
cold storage capacities (2.6 million lb and 1.0 million lb respectively),
but storage for aging is reportedly limited.
44.         Although bone and meat and bone meal are currently produced by
the "export" abattoirs, blood is not generally utilized. Similarly, little
attention has been paid to the quality of hides. The municipal slaughter-
houses have no facilities for by-product conversion.
45.         The three principal slaughterhouses operate throughout the year
but there is a significant seasonal fluctuation in deliveries. Average daily
deliveries to one typical plant varied between about 50 head in May to 250
head in November. The period of low deliveries -- the dry season -- also
coincides with the time of lowest carcass weight, resulting in even more ex-
treme variations in monthly production.
46.         Local Market.  Increased beef exports have not resulted in reduced
local sales but have led to a change in the types of meat offered on the local
market. Statistics suggest that most steer beef is being exported and that
more of cow beef and edible offals is being offered to the local market.  Much



ANNEX 3
Page 1 1
of the trade remains in the hands of small retail butchers,, who are licensed
individually to purchase cattle and who utilize the facilities of the munici-
pal abattoirs for slaughter. Sales are made either on a house-to-house basis
or in local markets. Meat supplies for the more sophisticated urban consumers
generally come from the export slaughterhouses, channeled through supermarkets.
47.         Local consumption in 1972 was equivalent to some 46% of gross pro-
duction, and, given the present relationship between prices of beef and other
meats, demand is likely to remain strong.  With current rates of population
increase of the order of 2.5 to 3% per year and per capita income growth of
4 to 5%, local demand may be assumed to increase by at least 5% per year and
absorb an increase of about 2% per annum in gross national beef production.
48.          Export Market.  Most exports are handled directly by the individ-
ual export meatworks. Higher prices for livestock in Costa Rica have also
reportedly resulted in an unofficial export of animals on the hoof, estimated
by some sources to be as high as 20,000 head per year.
49.          Beef exports to the US have been in the form of boneless lean meat,
which qualifies for import to the US under the "quota system". Under this
system of voluntary restraint, each supplying country agrees not to export to
the US more than a specified quantity of certain qualities of beef, the quan-
tity or "quota" being set annually by the US Government. By late 1972, how-
ever, this system had been waived in order to lessen price rises in the US.
50.         Maximum quota imports by the US remained unchanged at 1.16 billion
lb in 1970 and 1971, but Nicaragua's share increased by over 10%, from 41
million lb to 45.5 million lb (Table 7). Actual ex-ports exceeded the quota
by 1.2 million lb in 1970 but, owing to minor technical difficult'-es, fell
short by 2.0 million lb  in 1971.  Certain types of beef (particularly consu-
mer size cuts) may enter the US market outside the quota system, but are sub-
ject to an import tax of 10% ad valorem. Nicaragua has found this an attrac-
tive market and has developed these exports significantly in the last two
years. Some 8 million lb of non-quota meat were exported in 1972. The ex-
port value of beef in 1971 was about US$29 million, up by over US$2 milllon
over 1970.
51.         At present, beef sales are generally handled for NIcaraguan ex-
porters at the port of entry by meat brokers and customs brokers, though it
is understood that one Nicaraguan enterprise will soon opern its own agency
in Miami.  Contract sales are very limited, and only a small amount of pre-
mium quality meat is sold under established brand names.
52.       Forecasts by FAO, OECD, and the Bank anticipate a suIbstantial
increase in the demand for beef in Europe and the United States in the
1970's which is not likely to be satisfied from the expected increase in
local production. According to FAO forecasts, net imports to Europe in 1980
will have risen by some 120% over the 1970 level, while rates of. increase
approaching 10% per year are likely to be recorded for imports into the
United States between 1970 and 1980 (Table 8). These projections may prove



AMEX 3
Page 12
s^- tim-stic  i  domestic Droductiort is larger and populatio-n growth less than
'.sumed by FAC but, on the other hand, recent trends in beef prices and
un-oreseen consumer demand are highly encouraging signs.
53.          If Nicaraguan beef continues to compete successfully irn terms of
both price and quality with other foot-and-mouth free exporters, there is
little doObt that all increases in productioni, surplus to the demands of the
local market, could be marketed through established outlets in the United
States.  In the interests of diversification, however,, corsideration sboul.d
be given to extend exports to other markets such as the Caribbean and.
possibly, Japan.

Milk Marketing
59.         Processing Plants.  Milk marketing in Nicaragua is dominated by
the pasteurizing plants which handle much of the fluid milk supplied to the
Managuan market. Such plants, operating under the general control of the
"Oficina Coordinadora de la Industria Lactea", accept about 120,000 liters
per day, of which about 90,000 liters are sold as fluid milk. In September
1969 an additional plant (PROLACSA), designed to produce powdered milk,
particularly for export to other countries within the CACM, started operation
at Matagalpa, about 120 km northeast of Managua. Daily intake by this
plant currently ranges from 30,000 liters in the dry season to 70,000 liters
in the wet season. In December 1972, the liquid milk market of Managua
_          was disrupted and one plant destroyed.
60.         The Managua Market.  Annual supplies of milk to the processing
plants in Managua almost doubled, from 21.4 million liters in 1964 to 42.4
million liters in 1971. During the same period, there was a similar in-
crease in the number of farms supplying the factories, from 125 in 1964 to
271 in 1971. Over 40% of the supplies are drawn from farms in the Department
of Managua, and a further 50% is collected in the Pacific Zone; the remainder
is drawn from the region around Boaco. Average annual supplies per producer
range from 225,000 liters around Managua to 50,000 liters in the region of
Boaco. The Managua market is temporarily disorganized as a result of the
earthquake and some producers have abandoned milking and others have diverted
their supplies to PROLACSA.
61.         Milk received by the plants is either pasteurized and sold as
fresh milk or used for industrial purposes. One plant produces dry skim
milk, exporting 500 tons annually.  There are no regulations governing but-
terfat contents. A summary showing the growth of milk receipts by the Mana-
gua plants, and reported distribution between fluid and industrial uses is
shown below:

62.          Supplies to the pl1ants increased approximately 4 Žtl1iort litea>,
per year between 1964 and 1969 but this increase was not sustained into 1970
and 1971. The drought of 1971-72 has further reduced supplies and the earth-
quake in December 1972 has had serious consequences.
63.         The quantity of milk sold directly to consumers by producers is
tending to increase in proportion to milk sold to the plants in the Hanagua
district, not only because of the current processing and distribution probla.z
but also because of the better prices received.  In February 1973, predueers
were receiving between C$1.00 and C$1.20 per liter for direct consw er sales
as compared with from C$0.75 to CS0.80 per liter for sales to the plants6
Consumer preference is also for untreated milk with a higher fat content
than can be obtained from the plants.
64.          It would not be unreasonable to expect the market in Managua and
adjoining rural areas to grow at a rate of at least 5% per aexrium, the rate
at which population there is reportedly increasing, since such a rate would
be below that recorded for average pasteurized milk sales between 1964 and
1971 (about 87). If sales of pasteurized milk were to continue to increase
at 8% per annum over the 1971 level of 33 million liters, the demand may rise
to 45 million liters in 1975 and 66 vaillion liters in 1980, depending on t'ne
recovery of the market after the earthquake.
65.         As a result of the technical and management improvements intro-
duced recently on many of the farms supplying milk to the processing plants,
it is expected that part of the increased requirements would be met by rais-
ing productivity within existing herds. For the production to be doubled,
as required, however, some investment will also be necessary both to increase
the productive capacity of the existing herds and possibly to establish
additional specialized dairy herds.
PROLACSA and che Export Market
65.          The PRCLACSA plant which was established in 1967 in Matagalpa,
is equipped to produce dried whole milk and some specialized milk products.             0
Current daily receipts range from 30,000 liters in the summer to 70,000



ANNEX 3
Page 15
liters in the winter, accounting for a total intake, in 1971, of 13 million
liters and 21 million liters in 1972. Present maximum daily intake capacity
of 88,000 liters (25,000 US gallons), is intended to be enlarged to 200,000
liters per day within the next year if milk receipts can be increased suffi-
ciently.
67.         Though the region served by the plant includes most of the Central
:          Zone of Nicaragua, most of the milk is being collected by the plant in the
immediate vicinity of Matagalpa and from Boaco where roads and rainfall
conditions are good, and the company intends to intensify its operations.
68.         Supplies are drawn from about 350 producers.  Farms are relatively
small, and except on a few specialized units, milk is a by-product of beef
breeding. Average production is 1.8 liters per cow per day, with approximately
48% fat and 9.17% solids not fat.  Throughout the Central Zone, calving
rates are low (40-45%) and calf and adult mortality high. Cattle handling
and milking facilities are poor and, until recently, access to suppliers' farms
was extremely difficult. A road construction program (partly financed by
USAID) has resulted in the development of some 340 km of all-weather road
in the region to facilitate milk collection as well as effective technical
assistance. A group of 10 technicians provides extensive assistance to
farmers in the area. A US$2.6 million loan from IDB to INFONAC for on-farm
aevelopment in the region is being slowly disbursed owing to INFONAC's lack
of counterpart funds, and because of certain conditions restricting the use
of the loan proceeds.
69.         Export Market.  Powdered milk produced by PROLACSA is marketed
throughout Central America. Approximately 12% of current annual production
is sold in Nicaragua and the rest is exported under the provision of the
General Treaty for Central American Economic Integration. The Permanent
Secretariat makes an annual assessment of regional requirements for all types
of dried milk and expected production, allocates the disposal of surpluses
within the region, licenses imports from outside the region, and establishes
import duties.
70.         At present, the two largest producers of dried whole milk are
Nicaragua and Costa Rica, but as local consumption absorbs most of Costa
Rica's production (Table 9), Nicaragua accounts for some 75% of the volume
traded within the region. The largest importers of dried milk in the region
are El Salvador and Guatemala. Honduras is also a major importer but
it is currently not participating tuily in the CACM and is purchasing most
of its needs on the world market. Imports of dried whole milk from outside
the region in 1970 totaled almost 5.2 million kg and exceeded local pro-
duction by almost 40%.
71.         There are good prospects for increased demand for whole milk
powder within the region, but it will not be satisfied by projected increases
in production resulting from current livestock investment programs
in El Salvador, Guatemala, and Honduras, which are establishing their own



ANNEX 3
Page 16
powdered milk industries. Costa Rica is expected to increase its exports
of dried milk but this would not totally exclude the expansion of Nicaraguan
production to at least double the current level. The prospects for marketing
a wider range of processed milk products are also considered good by PROLACSA,
provided the quality of milk received is improved to the extent necessary
for such production.
72.         Prices.  The basis for price fixing by the processing plants was
established in 1963 and it provides incentives for the production of milk
in the dry season when supplies fall to a relatively low level. It also
recognizes the need to tailor production as closely as possible to the demand
of the fluid milk market, by passing on to the producer the lower    -
returns attributable to sales of "industrial" milk surplus to the needs of
the fluid market.
73.         In the dry season, all producers receive the same price, which
is fixed weekly as the average price payable for industrial and fluid milk,
weighted according to the volumes utilized for each purpose. During the
wet season, the producer is paid at the fluid milk price for that proportion
of total weekly milk he delivers which corresponds to the proportion of the
milk he delivered to the plants during the dry season to the total delivered
by him;  all additional milk is payable at the industrial milk price.
74.         Prices for dried whole milk on the regional market are above
world market levels, being protected by import duties of approximately 25%           i
as well as by the quota restrictions on imports. In 1970 wholesale prices
for various brands packed in 1-lb containers ranged from US$1,300 to
US$1,850 per ton. The highest prices were paid for the two brands produced
by PROLACSA, whether imported from Nicaragua or from outside the region.
Given the current trends in world market prices for dried milk, no fall in the
price paid within the CACM is expected.
