ANNEX 4
Page 1
NICARAGUA
AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Irrigated Crops
Introduction
1.        The pattern of agricultural development in. Nicaragua is highly
dualistic (Annex 1).  The modern farming sector, consisting of medium- and
large-size holdings, produces the bulk of the export crops (cotton, coffee,
sugarcane, bananas and tobacco) as well as irrigated rice, using sophisticated
techniques. Thanks largely to the entrepreneurial spirit of modern farmers
in the Pacific Zone and neighboring areas, there has been over the last two
decades a rapid increase in the production and export of these crops.
2.        Although accounting for the bulk of the use of agricultural
machinery, fertilizers, pesticides and short-term crop credit available in
Nicaragua, these modern farms are mostly owned by businessmen who employ
foremen for day-to-day supervision of farm operations. Such owners prefer
single cropping of an established crop to mixed rotational cropping, for
which higher husbandry standards would be required. Nevertheless, these
farmers are quick to take advantage of changes in both markets and production
techniques. Much of the land on which cotton is grown is leased on an annual
basis at rents varying with the cotton price.  This system facilitates ex-
pansion or contraction of the cultivated area and production in response to
world market price movements (Annex 1, Table 4).
3.        Farms of less than 7 ha constituting more than 50% of the total
number but occupying only 3.5% of the total area account for the bulk of the
production of Nicaragua's food crops (maize, beans, sorghum and upland rice)
which are grown with family labor under traditional methods and rainfed
conditions. Marketing only quantities surplus to their own consumption re-
quirements, these farms remain largely untouched by extension services and
average yields per hectare have shown only marginal improvement over the
past 10 years. Such farmers supplement their cash incomes by working on
larger farms during the cotton and coffee harvesting seasons. Because land
is not a limiting factor in Nicaragua, increased production of rainfed crops
comes from extension of the land farmed which, it is estimated, increased by
30% between 1963 and 1971.
4.        The existing crop production of the modern farm sector is already
largely capitalized. Much of this investment has been financed by long-term
lending programs, with supporting external finance mostly from IDB and USAID,
operated by the Banco Nacional de Nicaragua (BN), as evidenced by the bal-
ances of outstanding long-term loans by the BN as of December 31, 1971, set
out in the following table:



ANNEX 4
Page 2
Totals of
outstanding long-
term loans as of
Loan Classification                   December 31, 1971
C$       US$
t000    million
Irrigated rice                          35,615    5.1
Coffee                                  46,069    6.6
Agricultural Machinery                   4,556    0.6
(mainly for cotton)
In addition, the Instituto de Fomento Nacional (INFONAC) has largely financed
the development of bananas and tobacco.
5.        On the other hand, the expansion of traditional-type farming, while
demanding considerable infrastructure investment, calls more for short-term
crop loans than for longer term finance for on-farm investment. Moreover,
formulation of development priorities awaits completion of the current agri-
cultural sector survey being undertaken with USAID assistance. In these
circumstances, it is felt that irrigation will be the main source of demand
for medium- and long-term credit for crop production under the project, and
the possibilities in this field are examined in this annex.
Development of Irrigation in Nicaragua
6.        Irrigation in Nicaragua is found principally in the area west of
the Central Cordillera, where annual rainfall averages between 1,000 am
(39.37 inches) and 2,000 nn (78.74 inches), concentrated in the period of
May to December. There are, however, considerable variations in rainfall
amounts and distribution from year to year. In 1971, 41,000 ha (or approx-
imately 6% of the total cultivated area) were under irrigation. Run of the
river irrigation, used mostly for rice and sugarcane production, accounted
for 30,000 ha but as the rivers rising to the west of the Central Cordillera
are almost all non-perennial or have a low base flow, double cropping is
exceptional. Details of schemes initiated to promote irrigation development
are given below.
7.        Rivas Irrigation Project.  As originally prepared by the Instituto
de Fomento Nacional (INFONAC), this scheme was planned to pump water from
Lake Nicaragua to provide surface irrigation to 425 farms covering 9,000 ha.
Participating farmers were to be charged for water supplied at rates to cover
the costs of the operating agency (Empresa de Riego de Rivas). The original
estimate of total investment costs was C$34 million (US$4.9 million) and, on
this basis, the Bank approved a loan (332-NI) of US$2.6 million in 1963.
Subsequent investigations showed that the cost of the project works had risen
to US$6.3 million and could be justified only by high-value crops, for which
0



ANNEX 4
Page 3
there was insufficient data both as to production and marketing.  For this and
other reasons, the Bank's loan was reduced in 1966 by partial cancellation to
US$0.12 million.
8.        In 1969, financed partly by suppliers' credit, a revised version
of Stage I of the original project was implemented, with surface irrigation
replaced by a sprinkler system, utilizing raingun-type equipment, intended
for use with bananas and sugarcane.  Marketing difficulties have prevented
any expansion in banana production, and this, together with the difficulties
of using the equipment (which is owned and operated by the Empresa de Riego
de Rivas) on small farms and for other crops, has led to the present situation
where only 30 farmers covering some 900 ha are using the facility. Operating
at only 25% of its capacity and having considerable difficulty in collecting
water charges at the previously agreed rates, the Empresa de Riego de Rivas
incurs an annual loss of some C$3.5 million (US$0.5 million) whi-h is met by
a budget appropriation. The experience gained In the Rivas Irrigation Pro-
ject underlines the need for high-value crop production, for the irrigation
system to be designed to meet the requirements of these crops, and for assured
outlets for the commodities produced.
9.        Chinandega Banana Project.  This project to utilize groundwater in
the Chinandega area for the production of bananas for export was also started
by INFONAC. After encountering difficulties due to the variety grown and
INFONAC's attempts to handle, on its own, the marketing of the crop, the
project was taken over and revised by the Standard Fruit Company in 1969.
A variety more acceptable on the US market, with an anticipated yield of 40
m tons/ha, has been planted on 2,400 ha made up of farms of not less than 100
ha, and it is planned to extend production to 10,000 ha by 1974. Finance
for tubewell installation, pumps, sprinkler irrigation and planting material
have been provided, first by INFONAC and subsequently by the Standard Fruit
Company. This project also emphasizes the importance of ensuring that production
is geared to consumer demand and that marketing channels are adequate.
10.       Leon Irrigation Project.  As part of the cotton diversification
program, the Leon Irrigation Project (Proyecto de Riego de Leon - PRL) was
instituted by BN in 1969. Under this three-year program, loans are granted by
BN to selected farmers on preferential terms, including interest at 8% and re-
payment over 10 years with a two-year grace period, to cover the cost (C$312,
420, or US$44,630) of installing a standard sprinkler irrigation unit sufficient
to irrigate 123 manzanas (85 ha).  Under special arrangements made by the
Empresa Nacional de Luz y Fuerza (ENALUF), electricity consumed by these units
is charged at the rate of C$0.08 per kw, representing a reduction of 45% on
the normal tariff. Participating farmers can also obtain short-term credit
from BN at the concessionary interest rate of 12% p.a.
11.       The original program provided for 150 of these units in the northern
part of the Pacific Zone and to the north, south, and east of Lake Managua to
cover some 10,500 ha, but, by the end of 1972, only half had been installed



ANNEX 4
Page 4
and the prograin period was extended to the end of 1974, when it is hoped
that the original prograr, together with an additional 50 smaller units each
covering 50 manzanas (35 ha), will be completed. The total cost of the combined
program is C$85.1 million (US$12.2 million), of which C$60.0 million (US$8.6
million) is provided by loans from two British commercial banks, with the
equipment and technical assistance being supplied by a consortium of British
firims.
12.       On the 3,085 ha cropped under the program in 1972, production was
distributed as follows:
Permanent
Crops                       Annual Crops
Experi-
Plan-  Cot-        Sor-          Ground-  Sugar-           mental
Pastures tains  ton  Miaize ghum  Beans   nut        cane  Others  Farm    Tctal
…-- - - - - - -- - - - - - - ----  (ha)- --------…-- - - - - - ---- ----
1,354    323   925   208  124    70        31        81      62    77        3.255
Cropping intensity was only 105.5%.
13.       To assist in the development of alterriative crops through operation
of the project's experimental farm near Leon and to provide technical assistance
to participating farmers in irrigated farming methods, some five experts have
been provided by an Israeli firm (TAHAL) under a consultancy contract with the
Central Bank of Nicaragua (CB) and BN that expires in 1973.  The cost of these
services is not included in the project costs detailed above.
14.       The reasons for the slow rate of development of the program include.
(a)  technical difficulties such as volcanic dust in transforiners
and wind problems;
(b) high investment cost, which, on the larger unit, amounts to
nearly CS3,000 per manzana (US$600 per ha);
(c) the inflexibility of the system adopted under the prolect;
farmers are required to accept the standard unit of irrigation
equipment instead of being able to obtain a system tailored to
each farm's needs, including its present and potential
cropping pattern;
(d) the dominance of cotton in the project area and the difficulties
of finding either complementary or alternative crops. Planting
an additional crop after cotton involves giving up second and
third pickings and encounters much pest infestation. Research
and development on both production and marketing of alterna-
tive high-value crops is mostly in the preliminary phases;
(e) the absence of any real pressure on the land reduces the incen-
tive to move towards intensive production; and



ANNEX 4
Page 5
(f)  suitability of cotton production to the business farmer;
irrigated farming calls for higher standards of husbandry and
management on the farm.
15.       Irrigated Rice Project.  With the aid of IDB loans, BN has financed
investments in irrigation and farm machinery for rice production as well as
for rice processing plants. As a result, some 15,000 ha in the Pacific and
Central Zones belonging to about 50 farmers are now producing rice under
surface irrigation and largely mechanized cultivation. Of the water used,
75% comes from rivers or lakes and 25% from groundwater sources. Although
cropping intensity is only 117% and losses from pests (notably rats) are
considerable, yields of paddy average 3.9 m tons/ha.  This is double the yield
for rainfed paddy and reflects the use of higher yielding varieties, including
IRRI strains. Thanks to this program and the high support price paid by the
Instituto Nacional de Comercio Exterior y Interior (INCEI) (para 40), produc-
tion of milled rice in Nicaragua rose from 21,000 m tons in 1960/61 to 49.7
m tons in 1970/71. In the process, Nlicaragua's net import position (US$1.3
million in 1965) was converted into that of a net exporter (US$1.6 raillion in
1971). The results achieved by this program depended largely upon the use of
high yielding varieties and modern production techniques and the existence of
a protected market.
16.       Tobacco Development.  Production of Virginia, Sombreada, and
Burley-type tobacco has long been concentrated on the island of Ometepe in
Lake Nicaragua and in the departments of Esteli and Nueva Segovia.  Totalling
3.08 million lb in 1970/71, production is controlled by Tabacalera Nicaraguense
S.A. (TANIC) and utilized mainly in the local manufacture of cigarettes. Under
an INFONAC program and with external technical assistance, the production of
Havana tobacco is being promoted in the departments of Esteli and Nueva Segovia
where it is grown under irrigation on some 500 ha. Production in 1971 reached
1.8 million lb, of which 70% was exported to the USA. Recently, however, n.arket-
ing difficulties have been encountered there.  Average producer prices of C$11.48
per lb (US$1.6 per lb) were 4-1/2 times higher than those for blond toba.ceo In
addition to irrigation costs, investments financed by INFONAC cover curing
equipment, shade material, and agricultural machinery.
17.       Other Irrigation Schemes.  INFONAC and BN have also financed the
development of a number of individual irrigation schemes, mostly for irrigated
pastures. In addition, the possibility of a gravity irrigation scheme utiliz-
ing the partly regulated flow of the Rio Viejo has been examined by various
agencies since 1966.  It appears that such a scheme would cost i_$49 million
(US$7.0 million) to irrigate 6,300 ha, of which, 3,950 ha would be cropped
and 2,350 ha would be under pasture. With a cropping pattern of maize and
beans followed by groundnuts and sesame, together with cotton as an annual
crop, and a cropping intensity of 150%, the estimated rate of return is only
5%. To give a satisfactory rate of return, the cropping pattern would have
to include high-value crops for which outlets are limited or external markets
have yet to be developed.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
Water Resources                                                                          0
18.       As already indicated, reliable surface water resources from
river flows are limited and the boron content of Lake Managua renders it
unsuitable for irrigation use. No such restriction applies in the case of
Lake Nicaragua but the unhappy experience of the Rivas Irrigation Project
(paras 7 and 8) has deterred development of the use of this water resource.
19.       Although no comprehensive survey of the country's underground water
resources has been undertaken, investigations have confirmed the availabil:ty
of subterranean supplies in specific areas in the Pacific and Central Zones.
In particular, with UJNDP financial and technical assistance, a detailed study
of underground water resources in the Leon-Chinandega plain is nearing co-
pletion.  With a total area of 158,400 ha, this survey has showm that, without
depleting groundwater resources, up to 492 million m3 at an average depth of
13 m (42.6 ft) could be exploited annually to irrigate between 25,O00 and 566000
ha according to cropping pattern and water duties, and that there is sufficient
land of soil classes 1 and 2 in the area surveyed for such an expansion. Hiow'
ever, the fact that most of this land is already under cotton cultivation, .11
restrict the rate of irrigation development as experience with the Leon
Irrigation Project shows (para 14).
Water Legislation
20.       No water legislation is in effect in Nicaragua at present, al]t'houh
the UNDP project (para 19) has prepared a decree governing the extractio         A_E.
use of groundwater.  The main provisiaons of  this draft law, wheea may be
enacted in 1973, call for:
(a)  creation of a National Office for Underground Water
(Oficina Nacional de Aguas Subterraneas - ONAS);
(b)  all users of groundwater to be licensed by ONAS;
(c)  licenses to specify extraction rates and other conditions;
(d)  restriction of well drilling to firms registered with and
approved by ONAS;
(e)  drillers to furnish ONAS with results of their operations;
(f)  existing users of groundwater to be allowed to continue use,
subject to the provisions of the law, providing full details
to ONAS within one year; and
(Q) ONAS to be responsible for all matters concerning groundwater.
0



ANNEX 4
Page 7
O         21.       Decisions have yet to be reached regarding the constitution of ONAS
and the Ministry to which it will be responsible. No provision is made in the
draft law to levy charges on groundwater users.
Future Development of Irrigation in Nicaragua
22.       Expansion of the use of surface water is likely to be slow because
of the lack of reliable river flows and the high costs of schemes involving
river management or the utilization of the waters of Lake Nicaragua (paras
6 and 7).
23.       The prospects for the development of groundwater resources, partic-
ularly in the Leon - Chinandega plain at the northern end of the Pacific Zone
are basically good but are subject to the following constraints:
(a) the need for high-value crops and/or multiple cropping
patterns to justify the relatively high investment costs.
Increases in yield over rainfed production provide sufficient
benefits in the case of such crops as sugarcane, banana,
plantain, and rice (thanks to its protected price) but not in
the case of the other annual crops (cotton, maize, groundnuts,
and such) grown in the Pacific Zone;
(b) research into irrigated production requirements for new crops
under multiple cropping patterns is on an ad hoc basis and is
still in the preliminary phase.  Considerable insect infestation
and pest problems have been encountered;
(c) intensive production calls for higher standards of farm husbandry
and management than those necessary for established crops, such
as cotton and sugarcane. On the other hand, past experience
suggests that once the profitability of new crops and production
methods have been demonstrated, modern farmers in the area will
be quick to develop such opportunities; and
(d) for new crops, especially fruit and vegetables, there is a limit-
ed market in Nicaragua and main outlets must be sought in export
markets. In many cases, this calls for the provision of process-
ing facilities to match the expansion of production. The capa-
city of such processing plants and their power to fix producer
prices set limits to crop development.
24.       Taking all these factors into account, it is estimated that the
present rate of growth of irrigation is likely to be about 2,000 ha per year,
rising to about 3,000 ha per year when the program of the Leon Irrigation
Project is completed.

Irrigated Crops to be Financed Under the Project
25.       Traditional Export Crops.  Of the traditional export crops, only
sugarcane offers prospects for the limited expansion of irrigated production.
The total production of sugar in 1971 in Nicaragua was about 159,000 tons from
four large factories, together with an estimated production of 15,000 tons
of raw (brown) sugar from local plants. Distribution of cane production in
1970/71 was as follows:

26.       Each of the four large factories bases its operations on cane grown
on its own nucleus estate where, with intensive techniques and irrigation,
yields of 100 m tons/ha are obtained. These factories also buy cane from
neighboring small and medium size farms, where, under rainfed conditions, yields
range from 30 to 50 mn tons/ha.
27.       The largest factory at Chichigalpa processes 1,050,000 tons of cane
annually, of which 730,000 tons aregrown under irrigation on 7,500 ha on its
own estate and 320,000 tons produced mostly under rainfed conditions are
bought from some 200 outgrowers. To make fuller use of its existing process-
ing capacity, the factory is anxious to handle an additional 250,000 tons of
cane per year. Since in the factory area, the most suitable soils are already
planted with cane, expansion of production can be obtained only by increasing
outgrowers' yields on existing farms through irrigation.
28.       Of the total factory production of sugar in 1971, some 80,000 tons
were consumed in Nicaragua and 77,900 tons were exported under quota to the USA.
Internal consumption is expected to rise to 95,000 tons by 1980, and world
mIarket prospects have improved as indicated in the policy paper entitled
"Sugar - a reappraisal of investment policies for developing countries" dated
.November 7, 1972, prepared by the Commodities and Export Projections Division
of the Bank's Economic Analysis and Projections Department.

O         29.       Import Substitution Crops.  Apart from grains, limited opportunities
exist for increasing production of certain crops to be sold on the internal
market in substitution for imported supplies.  Examples of such crops are
plantains and onions.
30.       Nicaraguan production of plantains has failed to grow as fast as
demand over the last decade and the shortfall has been covered by imports
(mostly from CACM countries). These trends are evidenced by the figures
given in the following table:

31.       A large number of small farmers throughout the Pacific and Central
Zones grow plantain on small plots, primarily to meet their own needs, but
they market any quantity surplus to their requirements. Since production depends
on rainfall, the marketed surplus varies considerably from year to year, and
supply from this source is relatively inelastic. It is, however, being sup-
plemented by a growing number of irrigated production units, mostly about
50 manzanas (35 ha) in size,where yields of 20 m tons/ha and upwards are
achieved. Wind damage, particularly in the Rivas and Leon areas, is being
countered by planting windbreaks.
32.       The normal marketing channels are via middlemen/truckers, wholesalers
at the Managuan market or wholesaler/retailers at other markets to the urban
consumer. As a result of the earthquake of December 23, 1972, the importance
of Managua as the main consuming center has been temporarily reduced. There
has been no appreciable reduction, however, in overall demand, and marketing



ANNEX 4
Page 10
channels have swiftly adapted themselves to the changed territorial pattern
of consumption. From the table above, it will be seen that losses are esti-
mated at 15% production; this high level is due partly to the inherent dif-
ficulties of handling a perishable commodity under Niicaraguan climatic condi-
tions, but the situation is aggravated by the problems of collecting small
and variable quantities from scattered producers and the shortcomings in handling
and storage systems.
33.       Reliable and consistent data on producer prices are lacking and,
although imports help to stabilize price levels, there are considerable vari-
ations within a season and from year to year. Preliminary results of UNASEC
enquiries suggest that, over the past three years, average prices of plantain
for human consumption have been about C$500 (US$71.4) per m ton and for
animal feed about C$21 (US$3.0) per m ton.
34.       Similar trends are to be seen in the case of onions, as the following
table shows, although here, from a net export of 2,600 ql (118 m tons) in 1960,
Nicaragua had become a net importer of 21,100 ql (959 m tons) by 1972.

35.       Production comes mostly from small farmers utilizing run of the
river irrigation, notably in the Central Zone. The more progressive of these
import seed from the USA and obtain short-term credit from banks to cover the
cost of it, fertilizer and labor, most of which is employed at piece-rates
for specific operations.  Similar marketing channels to those for plantains
(para 32) operate for onions. Some producers sell their onions to merchants
"fat sight", in which case, it is the merchant's responsibility to harvest the
crop from the "banco' (a bed equal in area to one-tenth of a manzana). Ac-
cordina to UNASEC's estimates, average producer prices over the last three
years have been about C$70 per ql (US$220 per m ton). There are, however,



ANNEX 4
Page 11
considerable seasonal price fluctuations, with prices rising during the wet
season when supplies are short.  This is leading to trials by producers of
better keeping hybrid onions which could be preserved in simple wooden stores
without air conditioning to take advantage of higher market prices in May,
June and July.
36.       Attempts have been made to promote the production of kenaf by small
farmers in the department of Carazo as a substitute for imported jute used
by a sack factory at Diriamba.  BN made long-term credit available for the
provision of retting facilities but because of inadequate research, failure
to phase production, retting difficulties, the effect of the 1972 drought and
dissatisfaction with prices paid by the factory, the scheme has now been dropped.
37.       New Export Crops.  This is the area in which most developmental
effort has been focused and the difficulties that have been encountered in
promoting the production of bananas and tobacco have served to underline the
need for an assured market. With all the so-called "non-traditional" agricul-
tural exports, the smallness of the Nicaraguan market offers an insuIfficient
base for any large-scale production and the main outlets must be sought in
external markets. Since Nicaragua would appear to have little comparative
advantage in such production vis-a--vis the other members of the CACM, the
main attraction is the US market.
38.      ProminenL among potential "non-traditional" exports are various kinds
of fruit and vegetables. The presence of the Mediterranean fly in Nicaragua
poses added problems for any scheme involving the export to the US of fresh
fruit and vegetables. Although consideration has been given to the possibility
of establishing a USDA Quarantine Station in Nicaragua, better prospects seem.
to lie with the development of agro-industrial facilities for the export of
fruit and vegetables in processed or preserved forms.
39.       A successful pioneering enterprise on these lines has been estab-
lished at Chinandega which freezes 6 million lb of okra and 3 million lb of
peas and exports under contract to two large US supermarket chains. The fore-
cast value of exports in 1973 is US$500,000. Farmers with interests in the
factory account for the total present field supply but any expansion in out-
put would have to come from outgrowers Whio would need to invest in irrigation
facilities.  Under thte auspices of the Central Bank of Nicaragua, an inte-
grated enquiry into expansion prospects is now being undertaken. With AID
assistance (Proyecto Adelante), investigations into production possibilities
of other vegetables, such as cauliflower, broccoli and asparagus, are being made
at the Ministry of Agriculture's experimental irrigated farm near Leon, Over-
seas market prospects are being reviewed by EXPORTEMOS, an export promotion
agency, backed by the Chamber of Commerce, while technical processing problems
are being handled by the existing factory (AMOLONCA).  This coordinated
approach should provide a sound basis for further developments, but some time
must elapse before concrete conclusions can be drawn from the investigations
now proceeding.

supplies of vegetable oil and cake that would follow a reduction in the pro-
duction of seed cotton. At the present time, however, higher world market
prices have led to a recovery in cotton production and reduced the immediate
prospects for increasing oilseed production, which largely competes with
cotton for the same land in the Pacific Zone. In consequence, BN, which,
under a groundnut promotion scheme, had bought a number of shaker diggers for
hiring at rates based on cost, is unable to find users for this machinery.
Additionally, the largest of the two oilseed processing plants, which recently
established a 3,500-ha estate in Cosiguina to ensure supplies of groundnuts
to its factory at Chinandega, is revising its cropping program to increase
the area planted to cotton. In view of present price relationships vis-a-vis
cotton, there appears to be little immediate prospects for increasing oilseed
production, particularly under irrigation because of the level of investment
costs involved, although there may well be some opportunities for irrigated
seed production in certain areas.
Project Considerations
44.      General.  During the period of the project, it is estimated that an
additional 6,000/7,000 ha of land will be brought under irrigation in Nicaragua
(para 24).  Projected lending for irrigation for pastures and other crops under
the project would cover 5,150 ha, the balance being financed by farmers' own
resources or by other borrowing.
.=      45.       All sub-loans under the project would be separately appraised by
technical staff working under the direction of the Technical Director through
the Crop Specialist. Because the bulk of the sub-loans for both pastures and
other crops are expected to originate in the northern part of the Pacific Zone,
it will probably be necessary for the Crop Specialist to be located in the
Leon area.
46.      Appraisals of irrigated crop sub-loans will need to pay special
attention to water availability (including compliance with the new groundwater
law, when enacted), the standard of farm management, the need to minimize pest
and disease losses in both production and storage, the availability of techni-
cal assistance, and assurance of marketing outlets for the proposed crop
production. Technical assistance should come principally from the staff of BN,
at present engaged with the Leon Irrigation Project, and from the Ministry of
Agriculture's staff, currently involved in the Proyecto Adelante. As regards
marketing outlets, project lending will need to be carefully coordinated with
the program for agro-industrial development to be undertaken under a proposed
IDB loan to the Special Development Fund (Fondo Especial de Desarrollo) of the
Central Bank.
47.      The size of loan for irrigated crop production will be determ-ined
largely by such factors as the topography of the farm, the available water
supply, crop patterns and water duties and the most suitable irrigation system
(e.g., gravity versus sprinkler). For farms much smaller in size than those
shown in the models (20 ha for gravity irrigation for sugar and 35 ha for a
sprinkler system for plantain), investment costs per unit of land will be



ANNEX 4
Page 14
higher and the rate of return corresDondingly lower. On the other hand, there
may well be some lending for run-of-the-river projects, where both investment
costs and farm size could be lower.
48.       Investment would cover such items as land levelling, construction of
irrigation channels, establishement of windbreaks, land preparation and plant-
ing (in the case of perennial crops only), tractors, trailers and implements,
well construction, pump installation (including pumphouse), sprinkler systems,
where appropriate, and on-farm storage.  All items required under these head-
ings are readily available in Nicaragua.  Five firms (three in Managua and two
in Leon) are distributors of various makes of pumps, motors, and irrigation
equipment, all of which are imported freely.
49.       Apart from three rotary and nine percussion rigs operated by
Government agencies, in the commercial sector, there are three firms with,
in all, 20 percussion and four rotary drilling rigs. The largest company in
the field probably accounts for two-thirds of the total private drilling, in
addition to operating in Guatemala and Costa Rica. All steel well casings,
as well as steel columns and shafting, are imported.
50.       Farm Models.  In Appendixes 10--5 and 10-6, two illustrative farm
models for irrigated crops have been included, the first covering a 35-ha unit
growing plantain and the second portraying the conversion to irrigation of a
20-ha farm currently producing sugarcane under rainfed conditions. The plantain
model typifies a farm in the northern part of the Pacific Zone previously used
for rough grazing.  Investment is in land preparation and planting, machinery
and equipment, and a sprinkler irrigation system, based on a 60-ft well, 30-hp
electric motor, 4 inch main lines and 3 inch subsidiary lines, with 18 inch
sprinklers, designed to deliver some 350 mm per month additional water in a
16 hr pumping day, to meet the maximum additional water requirement during
the months of April and December. Investment costs in irrigation amount to
C$2,660 per manzana (US$540 per ha). Estimated yields (20 m tons/ha) are
within actual achievement of similar existing farms and have been conservatively
priced at C$317/fm tons allowing for 90% of the total output to be sold for human
consumption at C$350/m tons and the balance for animal feed at C$21m tons. On
these bases, after allowing for investment in replacement machinery in year 8,
and a 10-year life for the main irrication assets, the financial rate of return
is 25%. On the proposed normal lending terms (loan at 13% per annum to cover
80% of investments) and with a grace period of two years, farmer's cash flow
from year 2 onwards is satisfactory. The deficit in year 1 would be largely
covered by his contribution (20%) to investment costs, which a farmer of this
size should be able to provide from his own resources. The investment would
also create jobs for nine permanent and six casual workers.
51.       The sugarcane model is representative of a typical outgrower of the
Ingenio San Antonio at Chichigalpa in the department of Chinandega, who at
present is producing rainfed sugarcane on one-half of his 20-ha holding and
leases the remuaining 10 ha for pasture. To install gravity irrigation on



ANNEX 4
Page 15
the whole of his holding, it is assumed that this farmer would clear his
rainfed crop, plant the whole 20 ha with selected planting material supplied
by the factory, and, after the second year, commence partial replanting so
that his annual production and costs are the average of a five-year cycle, with
four r-toon cuts. Irrigation investment costs in well, pump, motor, and pump-
house, with capacity to supply in a 17-1/2 hr pumping day a maximum supplementary
water requirement equal to 310 mm/month, would amount to C$2,300 per manzana
(US$490 per ha). With average yields increasing from 40 to 93 tons/ha, the
financial rate of return over a 10-year life would be 34%. No provision is
made for machinery investment, as the factory supplies machinery services on
contract terms. Cutting would be by hand, with transport provided by the
factory. The assumed farm-gate price of C$42 per ton (US$6 per ton) is in line
with current contract prices to outgrowers. From two permanent and two casual
workers, labor requirements would rise to four permanent and six casual workers.
Such a borrower would probably be eligible for the special lending terms pro-
posed for small farmers (loan to cover 90% of investment costs and to bear
interest at 11% per annum). -On this basis, with a grace period of zwo years,
a satisfactory farmer's cash flow could be expected (Appendix 10-6).
52.      These two crops have been chosen for model purposes because they
are already produced under irrigated conditions in Nicaragua and there is an
assured outlet for the projected additional production. This outlet is, however,
limited and the model mix in the estimation of total project costs takes the
relative market opportunities into account.  However, the door should be left
0F         open for the financing of other crops under the project provided that appraisal
of such sub-projects gives satisfactory assurances as to both production and
markets, and, when prepared on the same basis as the models in Appendixes 10-5
and 10-6, show an adequate financial rate of return. At this stage, such crop
possibilities can only be indicated in general terms, as in paragraphs 34 to
43, and this stresses the importance that will need to be attached to vigorous
appraisal of all crop component sub-loans. Excluded from the project, however,
at this stage should be any proposed sub-loans involving solely: (a) cotton
or coffee production, in view of the policy of diversification for these crops
and the fact that their existing production is already largely capitalized
(para 4); and (b) rice and tobacco, since production expansion for these crops
has already been catered for by recent development programs (paras 15 and 16).
Under these flexible conditions, it is anticipated that there will be a coin-
siderable range in the levels of investment costs, although technilcal consider-
ations are likely to set the lower limit at about C$70,000 (US$10,G00) in the
case of irrigation loans where pumping is involved.
53.      When agricultural development priorities are formulated on the
completion of the ongoing sector survey analysis, the crop component should
be reviewed by the Technical Director with a view to defining more closely
(particularly for the benefit of participating banks) any new investment
opportunities that may have arisen in either irrigated or rainfed farming.
Any revision involving modifications of the exclusions detailed in paragraph
52 would, however, need to be referred to the Bank for prior approval,
0



ANNEX 4
Page 16
54.       These arrangements differ from those proposed by the identification
mission of the FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program, which visited Nicaragua in
FebruaryIMarch 1972. In its report (17/72 NIC 6 of June 20, 1972, this
mission recomunended that a separate agricultural credit project should be pre-
pared for implementation through the Special Development Fund of the Central
Bank of Nicaragua in the same manner as in the proposals for the Livestock
Credit Project prepared by the FAO/CP Program. Within such an agricultural
credit program, four crop enterprises were tentatively identified, viz.
irrigated sugar cane, together with three rainfed crops, groundnuts, castor
bean, and kenaf, while finance for further studies into oil palm development
in the Atlantic Zone and agricultural research reorganization was sugge$ted in
preparation for a second-phase project.  The amalgamation of the agricltural
and livestock proposals into one integrated project was discussed and agreed
with the Nicaraguan authorities following a pre-appraisal mission in July,
1972.  In the past in Nicaragua, long-tei<  credit has largely been availab.le
only as part of specific crop development programs. This project, as now
appraised, would stress the need for loans based on whole far-i developmnent plans
which have taken competing alternatives into account.  For example  investment
in irri.gation could be for beef fattening or dairy operations, or for crop
production involving single or multiple cropping. The alternative closera shoLuid
depend on the circumstances of each case rather than, be determined by the avail-
ability of a particular line of credit.  As regards individual crops suggested
by the FAO/CP identification mission, while irrigated sugarcane is retained as
an illustrative model, prospects for kenaf and oilseeds have changed consid.er-
ably (paras 36 and 43).  For these reasons, it is preferred to give maximum
flexibility in the definition of thie project crop component as indicated in
paragraphs 52 and 53.  Until agricultural development priorities are determlined
in the light of the current sector survey, it would seem premature to undertake
further studies of the kind proposed for oil palm 9, while, in the case of
agricultural research, a detailed study 'ias been recently made oy a National
Commission, with technical assistance from the Tastituto interacericaco de
Ciencias Agricolas (IICA) financed by IDS, arnd same of its recommendations are
already being adopted (Annex 1).
October 23, 1973

NICARAGUA
AGRTCULTIRAL CREDIT PROJECT
FONDO and the Participating Banks
A. The Special Development Fund
1.        The Special Development Fund (FONDO) was created by Presidential
Decree in April of 1972 to promote and diversify Nicaragua's production and
exports by providing complementary loans for programs within the production
sectors such as agriculture and livestock, industry, and fisheries. Loans
will be made available to the producers through private and Government-owned
financing institutions, which will discount or borrow from the FONDO, in
accordance with the regulations established for each specific program.
Administration
2.        The Central Bank of Nicaragua (CB) is the sole administrator of
the FONDO, while policy making has been entrusted to the Board of Directors
of CB. The Board is also responsible for the approval of (a) the organiza-
tional and regulatory matters of the FONDO; (b) the nrograms to be operated
through the FONDO; (c) the agreements to obtain resources; and (d)
the lines of credit to be granted to the financial institutions.
Capital Structure
3.        FONDO's canital would consist of  (a) special allocations made
by the Government for specific programs; (b) a proportion of the profits
made by the Central Bank every year;  (c) FONDO's own profits; and  (d) dona-
tions and concessions made to the FONDO. For its lending operations, the
FONDO may use its owm capital resources as well as any resources obtained
from international institutions, foreign institutions, or floating of bonds
abroad and resources obtained within the country through borrowing or floating
of bonds.  Permission to borrow and issiie bonds within the country and abroad
will have to be obtained by the FONDO from the Ministry of Finance. The
Government will be the Guarantor of any operations of this type.
Operations
4.        The FONDO is authorized to provide finance through loans or dis-
cotnts to Government-owned or private financial institutions which operate
programs for agriculture and livestock, fisheries, agro-industries, or any
other activity that increases production in Nicaragua. Loans granted by the
institutions financed by tlhe FONDO can extend to more than five years, as
authorized by Article 50 of the Banking Law.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
Accounts and Auditing
5.        FONDO's accounts will be kept separate from those of CB and audited
by the auditor of the CB and also be subject to review by the Superintendent
of Banks. An annual report on operations will be submitted to the President
of the Republic.
Programs
6.        While the FONDO does not yet have operating experience, the regu-
lations and procedures drawn un for two programs to be financed by IDB suggest
that the institutional framework would be satisfactory.  One of the IDB pro-
grams is for agro-industries (US$6.1 million) and the other, a program for
construction materials and activities (USS4.0 million). Lending procedures
envisage the opening of lines of credit to the private and Government finan-
cial institutions which will on-lend to the final beneficiaries. Financing
of the cost of individual sub-projects will be shared by FONDO, the benefi-
ciary, the financial intermediary, and IDB. Interest paid by the sub-borrower
will be distribuited to the FONDO, intermediary institutions, and IDB in
relation to the extent of their participation in the financing and the terms
agreed by IDB. Subsidiary loan agreements will be signed between FONDO and
the participating banks and between them and the beneficiaries.

8.        The projected financial statements of the FONDO (Annex 8) relate
only to the operations under the Agricultural Credit Project, except for the
inclusion of a pro-rata assessment of the common expenditures incurred by
the FONDO for all its programs. According to the cash flow and the earnings
projections, the FONDO will need Government contributions of about IJS$1.0
million during the period of loan disbursement under the project (Annexes
6 and 8).
Staffing
9.        The FONDO has a full-time Director, appointed by the President
of the Central Bank, who is responsible for the operations of the F6NDO. He
will be assisted by a small staff and by the project managers appointed in
agreement with the institutions that finance each program. The proposed
Bank project wouldhe executed by a special project unit of the FONDO which
wouldbe in charge of appraisal and supervision of the sub-loans to farmers.
The staff would be composed of a technical director, a co-director, a crops
specialist,and other project technicians. The technicians employed by the
participating banks would also serve under the technical direction of the
Technical Director.
B. Participating Banks
10.       The Banco Nacional de Nicaragua and the private commercial banks
wouldbe eligible to participate in the project, channeling resources from the
FONDO and their own funds to sub-borrowers by way of loans for eligible pur-
poses in conformity with the terms and conditions of the project.
Banco Nacional de Nicaragua
11.       BN was incorporated in 1912 in Hlartford, Connecticut, to serve as
a commercial bank and to issue currency for Nicaragua. In 1924, the Govern-
ment of Nicaragua took over the bank and gave it additional functions, and
in 1940, it became the trading agent for the Government. Then, in 1961, the
Central Bank of Nicaragua and the Institute for Internatio,al Trade (INCEI)
were organized and took over BN's central banking and trading functions. In
1968, BN's organic law was again amended to expand its development bank
functions and to give more executive powers to its management.
0

ANNEX 11

NICARAGUA
AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Economic Rate of Return Calculations
1.        The rates of return from the project to the economy have been
calculated separately for each farm model, as shown in Appendix 11-1, on
the following assumptions:
I.   Prices
(i) Beef Development: The prices shown below and used for
farm models (Appendixes 10-3 and 10-4) were reduced by
5% to allow for the impact of possible decline in world
beef prices.
(a)  Cull bulls were priced at ¢1,500 (US$215) for years
O to 5 and at ¢ (US$243) from project year 6 onward,
assuming higher liveweight as a result of improved
techniques.  These prices correspond to US$0.45 to
US$0.49 per kg. liveweight, according to the size
of the animal, and are those paid currently by the
local slaughterhouses who export to the U.S. market;
(b)  Cull cows were priced at ¢830 (US$119) for years
O to 5 and at ¢850 (UTS$121) from year 6 onward. The
average price per kg. liveweight is ¢2.50 (US$0.36);
(c)  HIeifers 24-36 months were priced at 0700 (US$100)
for years 0 to 5 and at ¢1,000 (US$143) from year
6 onward. The liveweight varies between 300 and
350 kg.;
(d)  Steers between 24 and 36 months were priced at
t1,130 (US$161) for years 0 to 5 and at e1,270
(US$180) from year 6 onward.  For steers of more
than 36 months the price assumed for year 0 to 5
was 01,270 (US$181).
(ii) Dairy Development: A reduction of 10% was made in the
average sale price of 00.80 (US$0.11) per liter which
had been assumed for farm models (Appendixes 10-1 and
10-2) on the basis of the prices paid by local consumers
on retail sales and those paid by the processing factories.



ANNEX 1 1
Page 2 of 2
(iii) Irrigated Crops: The prices assumed for sugarcane were
5% less than the prevailing U.S. Preferential f.o.b.
prices.
(iv) Current domestic market prices were used for pastures
and plantains, which are regarded as non-tradeable
commodities.
(v)  Current price levels have been used for investment and operat-
ing costs wqith due allowances for prevailing taxes and customs
duties on imported items as shown below:
Other Implements
Including Irriga-   Fertilizer and
Tractor   tion Equipment        Insecticides
Import duty           7%           5%                   5%
Other taxes
charged on CIF
value and duty       2.2%         2.2%                 2.2%
(vi)  Salvage values proportional to unexpired asset life have
been included; and
(vii)  The demand for labor in rural areas shows a seasonal pattern
with peaks in July-September and December-January which
correspond, respectively, to cultivation of basic grains and
harvesting of cotton, coffee and sugarcane. On the other
hand, in the months of lowest demand, 20 to 35 percent of
available labor is underutilized. On the basis of the avail-
able information in regard to seasonal fluctuations in employ-
ment, the widespread under employment on subsistence farms and
the fact that, a large part of the labor to be employed on
the Project would be unskilled, labor has been costed at 50%
of the prevailing rates.
Economic Rate of Return
2.        When weighted according to the relative share of various models in
the total project cost, the overall economic rate of return of the Project is
25%.
October 23, 1973
